Salah — Prayer
beginner
8 min
Sunnah Recitation Lengths in the Five Obligatory Prayers
Ruling with proof on recitation lengths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah recitation length for each of the five daily prayers and apply the division of al-Mufassal surahs to determine appropriate recitation.

Core Idea
Recitation lengths are sunnah, not fard—the Prophet’s practice guides the length, not an obligatory command.

The Hanafi school, based on the Prophet's established practice, recommends particular lengths of Qur'anic recitation for each obligatory prayer. This lesson details those lengths and the classification of surahs into long, medium, and short. Understanding these guidelines helps the worshiper achieve the optimal form of prayer without exceeding the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify prayer and recommended length
State the sunnah recitation length for each of the five prayers: Fajr long, Zuhr long, 'Asr medium, Maghrib short, 'Isha medium.
2
Learn division of Mufassal
Memorize the three categories: Tiwal (long) from Hujurat (49) to Buruj (85), Awsat (medium) from Buruj to Bayyinah (98), Qisar (short) from Bayyinah to end.
This classification is specific to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Recognize sunnah not fard
Understand that these lengths are sunnah muakkadah (emphasized recommendation) based on the Prophet's action (fi'l), not an imperative command. A valid prayer may have any recitation.
Hanafi usul: action of the Prophet indicates istihbab.
4
Review hadith proofs
For each prayer, note the hadith evidence: Fajr (Surah al-Tur, Qaf, Takwir), Zuhr (Surah al-Sajdah), 'Asr (al-Buruj, al-Tariq), Maghrib (al-Ikhlas, al-Kafirun), 'Isha (al-Shams).
These are ahad hadith, zanni in transmission.
5
Apply the rule of necessity
In necessity (e.g., travel), recitation may be shortened to 'according to the situation' (بقدر الحال). This is an exception to the sunnah lengths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الفجر، والظهر، وأوساطه في العصر والعشاء وقصاره في المغرب، ومن الحجرات طوال المفصل إلى البروج، ومنها أوساطه إلى لم يكن، ومنها قصاره إلى الآخر، وفي الضرورة بقدر الحال، وكره توقيت سورة للصلاة أي تعيين سورة للصلاة بحيث لا يقرأ فيها إلا تلك السورة. قوله: في الفجر؛ لما ثبت عنه أنه قرأ فيه سورة: والطور (أخرجه البخاري)، وسورة: إذا الشمس كورت وسورة ق (أخرجه مسلم). قوله: والظهر؛ لما أخرج مسلم عن أبي سعيد الخدري قال: كنا نحزر قيام رسول الله في الظهر والعصر فحزرنا قيامه في الأوليين من الظهر قدر الم تنزيل السجدة. قوله: في العصر والعشاء؛ لما ثبت أنه لو قرأ في العصر: والسماء ذات البروج والسماء والطارق (أخرجه أبو داود)، وقرأ في العشاء بسورة: والشمس وضحاها (أخرجه النسائي). قوله: في المغرب؛ لما ثبت أنه قرأ في المغرب: قل يا أيها الكافرون وقل هو الله أحد (أخرجه ابن ماجة). قوله: وفي الضرورة؛ عطف على قوله: في السفر، أو قوله: في الحضر. قوله: وكره؛ توقيت هذه الكراهة إنما فيما لم يثبت من الشارع، وأما التعيين بما ثبت تعيينه من الشارع فلا كراهة فيه، بل هو مسنون، كما ثبت أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقرأ في فجر الجمعة: الم تنزيل السجدة وهل أتى على الإنسان (أخرجه الشيخان). قوله: بحيث؛ أشار به إلى أن مطلق التعيين ليس بمكروه، بل التعيين الدائمي والالتزامي لما فيه من هجر الباقي، والتزام ما لم يعهد في الشرع التزامه المورث إلى فساد اعتقاد العوام، حيث يظنونه لازما، لا سيما إذا صدر من المقتدى.
In Fajr, and Zuhr, and medium-length in 'Asr and 'Isha, and short in Maghrib. And from al-Hujurat to al-Buruj are the long of al-Mufassal, and from them to 'Lam Yakun' are the medium-lengths, and from them to the end are the short. And in necessity, according to the situation. And it is disliked to fix a surah for a prayer, meaning to assign a specific surah to a prayer such that only that surah is recited. His statement: 'In Fajr'; because it is established that he (peace be upon him) recited in it Surah al-Tur (related by al-Bukhari), and Surah Idha al-Shamsu Kuwirat and Surah Qaf (related by Muslim). His statement: 'In Zuhr'; because Muslim related from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'We estimated the standing of the Messenger of God in Zuhr and 'Asr, and we estimated his standing in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr as the length of Alif Lam Meem Tanzil al-Sajdah.' His statement: 'In 'Asr and 'Isha'; because it is established that he recited in 'Asr: 'Wa al-Samai Dhat al-Buruj' and 'Wa al-Samai wa al-Tariq' (related by Abu Dawud), and he recited in 'Isha: 'Wa al-Shamsi wa Duhaha' (related by al-Nasa'i). His statement: 'In Maghrib'; because it is established that he recited in Maghrib: 'Qul Ya Ayyuha al-Kafirun' and 'Qul Huwa Allahu Ahad' (related by Ibn Majah). His statement: 'And in necessity'; this is connected to his statement: 'in travel' or his statement: 'in residency'. His statement: 'And it is disliked'; the dislike of this fixing is only regarding what has not been established from the Lawgiver. As for assignment that is established by the Lawgiver, there is no dislike in it; rather it is sunnah, as it is established that the Prophet used to recite in Fajr of Friday: 'Alif Lam Meem Tanzil al-Sajdah' and 'Hal Ata 'ala al-Insan' (related by al-Bukhari and Muslim). His statement: 'such that'; he indicated by this that absolute assignment is not disliked, rather permanent and obligatory assignment, because it involves abandoning other surahs and adhering to what was not customarily adhered to in the Law, leading to corruption of the common people's belief, as they think it is obligatory, especially if it comes from a leader.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses two related topics: the sunnah lengths of recitation in the obligatory prayers, and the ruling on permanently assigning a specific surah to a particular prayer. The matn states the rulings concisely: recitation in Fajr and Zuhr should be long (with long surahs), in 'Asr and 'Isha medium-length, and in Maghrib short. Then it defines the divisions of al-Mufassal surahs: from Surah al-Hujurat to al-Buruj are the long ones, from al-Buruj to 'Lam Yakun' are medium, and from there to the end of the Qur'an are short. In cases of necessity (such as travel), one may recite according to the situation. Finally, it states that it is makruh (disliked) to fix a specific surah for a prayer, meaning to habitually recite only that surah. The commentary then provides hadith proofs for each ruling. For Fajr, it cites that the Prophet recited Surah al-Tur, Surah al-Takwir, and Surah Qaf. For Zuhr, it reports that his qiyam was about the length of Surah al-Sajdah. For 'Asr and 'Isha, it mentions Surah al-Buruj, Surah al-Tariq, and Surah al-Shams. For Maghrib, it mentions the two short surahs: al-Kafirun and al-Ikhlas. The commentary then clarifies that the prohibition of tawqeet (fixing a surah) applies only to those surahs not specifically assigned by the Prophet. If the Prophet himself assigned a surah for a particular prayer (like the two surahs in Fajr on Friday), then that is sunnah, not makruh. The commentary further explains that the dislike is for permanent and obligatory commitment, as it leads to abandoning other Qur'anic passages and causing common people to mistakenly believe that it is obligatory. Thus, the Hanafi position is that the lengths are sunnah, and fixing a surah beyond what is established in the Sunnah is makruh tanzihan.

Key Terms
طوال المفصل (tiwal al-mufassal): The long surahs of al-Mufassal, from Surah al-Hujurat (49) to Surah al-Buruj (85).
أوساطه (awsatuhu): The medium-length surahs of al-Mufassal, from al-Buruj (85) to 'Lam Yakun' (i.e., Surah al-Bayyinah 98).
قصاره (qisaruhu): The short surahs of al-Mufassal, from 'Lam Yakun' (Surah al-Bayyinah) to the end of the Qur'an.
توقيت سورة (tawqeet surah): Permanently assigning a specific surah to be recited in a specific prayer, to the exclusion of other surahs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the sunnah recitation length for 'Asr prayer?
A) Long (Tiwal al-Mufassal)
B) Medium (Awsat al-Mufassal)
C) Short (Qisar al-Mufassal)
D) No specific sunnah

Key Takeaway
Recitation in prayer has recommended lengths based on the Prophet's practice: long for Fajr and Zuhr, medium for 'Asr and 'Isha, short for Maghrib.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
10 min
The Ruling on Permanently Assigning a Specific Surah to a Prayer (Tawqeet)
Ruling with proof on tawqeet (fixing a surah)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on tawqeet (fixing a surah) by distinguishing between sunnah-assigned surahs and arbitrary permanent assignment, and identify when it becomes makruh tanzihi.

Core Idea
Fixing a surah is only disliked when done permanently for a prayer without prophetic precedent; following the Prophet's assignments is sunnah.

The Hanafi school teaches that while it is commendable to often recite certain surahs, permanently binding oneself to a specific surah for a particular prayer is disliked (makruh tanzihi) unless the Prophet himself did so. This lesson clarifies the scope of that ruling and the wisdom behind it.

Guided Path
1
Define tawqeet
Tawqeet means permanently assigning a specific surah to a particular prayer so that only that surah is recited in that prayer.
2
Identify the ruling
The ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked), not haram. This is because the practice is not forbidden by a decisive text.
3
Recognize the exception
If the assignment is established by the Prophet (e.g., reciting Surah al-Sajdah and al-Insan in Fajr on Friday), it is sunnah, not makruh.
The exception is explicitly mentioned in the commentary.
4
Understand the reason
Permanent assignment leads to abandoning other surahs and may cause common people to believe the practice is obligatory (fard), which is corrupting to their belief.
The reasoning is preventive (sadd al-dhara'i').
5
Apply to scenarios
Distinguish between following the Prophet's assignment (sunnah) and innovating a new permanent assignment (makruh). Consider the role of the imam and the potential misconception of the congregation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is the exceptional case where permanently fixing a surah is not makruh but sunnah?
A) When it is a short surah
B) When the Prophet himself assigned it to that prayer
C) When the imam decides
D) When it is done only once

Key Takeaway
Fixing a surah is disliked only when it becomes a permanent exclusive practice not established by the Prophet; following the Prophet's example is commendable.

Recitation Lengths and Dislike of Permanently Specifying a Surah
Ruling on recommended recitation lengths and the dislike of permanently specifying a surah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended recitation lengths in Maghrib and explain why permanently specifying a surah without Shari'ah basis is makruh tanzih.

Core Idea
Permanently specifying a surah for prayer is makruh unless based on the Sunnah.

In this passage, the author provides guidance on the length of recitation in Maghrib prayer and then addresses the issue of permanently binding oneself to a specific surah. Understanding this ruling helps avoid rigid practices that may lead to neglect of other Quranic passages and confusion among the laity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended recitation length for Maghrib
The text states that in Maghrib one should recite the short surahs from the end of al-Mufassal (qisar). Locate this ruling and note the division of al-Mufassal into long, medium, and short sections.
The division is based on practice of the Prophet.
2
Recognize the main ruling: dislike of tawqit surah
The author says 'It is disliked to specify a surah for prayer,' meaning to permanently commit to reciting only that surah in a particular prayer. Identify the precise scope: it is tawqit (binding specification) that is disliked, not mere preference.
The text clarifies that absolute specification is not disliked, only permanent and binding.
3
Examine the dalil from the Prophet’s practice
The text provides evidence: the Prophet recited specific surahs in Maghrib (Ibn Majah) and in Friday Fajr (Bukhari/Muslim). It also cites the incident of Mu'adh, where the Prophet discouraged him from reciting long surahs. Understand that these practices establish sunnah recommendations, not rigid obligations.
The dalil shows when specification is sunnah and when it is makruh.
4
Understand the commentator’s distinction
The disclaimer states that the dislike applies only when the Shari'ah has not specified that surah. If the Shari'ah has specified (e.g., Friday Fajr surahs), then it is sunnah. This distinction is key to applying the ruling correctly.
The commentator (of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah) provides this refinement.
5
Note the rationale for the dislike
Permanent specification leads to abandoning other Quranic recitations and may cause common people to believe the act is obligatory (fard). The Prophet's warning to Mu'adh highlights the risk of causing fitnah. This is why it is makruh.
The reason is to protect the integrity of worship and the understanding of the laity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقصاره في المغرب، ومن الحجرات طوال المفصل إلى البروج، ومنها أوساطه إلى لم يكن ، ومنها قصاره إلى الآخر، وفي الضرورة بقدر الحال، وكُرِهَ توقيتُ سورة للصلاة : أي تعيين سورة للصلاة بحيث لا يقرأ فيها إلا تلك السورة. قوله : في المغرب ؛ لما ثبت أنه قرأ في المغرب : قُلْ يَتَأَيُّهَا الْكَفِرُونَ * و قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحدٌ ، أخرجه ابن ماجة، وقد وردت في هذه الأبواب أخبار كثيرة بسطناها في «السعاية». قوله : وفي الضرورة ؛ عطف على قوله : «في السفر»، أو قوله : «في الحضر». قوله : وكره ; توقيت هذه الكراهة إنّما فيما لم يثبت من الشارع، وأما التعيين بما ثبت تعيينه من الشارع فلا كراهة فيه، بل هو مسنون، كما ثبت أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقرأ في فجر الجمعة : الم تنهل - السجدة ، وهَلْ أَتَى عَلَى الإِنسَينِ أخرجه الشيخان وغيرهم، وفي المقام تفصيل وتحقيق، فرغنا عنه في «السعاية». قوله : بحيث ; أشار به إلى أنّ مطلق التعيين ليس بمكروه، بل التعيين الدائمي والالتزامي لما فيه من هجر الباقي، والتزام ما لم يعهد في الشرع التزامه المورث إلى فسادِ اعتقاد العوام، حيث يظنونه لازماً، لا سيما إذا صدر من المقتدى. معاذ ، فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أتريد أن تكون فتاناً يا معاذ إذا أعمت الناس فاقرأ بـ وَالشَّمس وضحها وسبع اسْمَ رَبِّكَ الْأَعْلَى ) و وَالَّيلِ إِذَا يَغْشَى ) وأَقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ .
Recitation lengths: in Maghrib (recite) the short ones (qisar). From 'al-Hujurat' to 'al-Buruj' are the long ones of al-Mufassal; from there to 'Lam Yakun' are the medium ones; from there to the end are the short ones. And in necessity, according to the situation. And it is disliked to specify a surah for prayer, i.e., to designate a surah for prayer such that one recites only that surah. His statement: 'in Maghrib' because it is established that he recited in Maghrib: 'Say, O disbelievers' and 'Say, He is Allah, the One' — reported by Ibn Majah. And there are many reports on these topics which we have expanded in 'al-Sa'ayah'. His statement: 'and in necessity' is connected to his statement 'in travel' or his statement 'in residence'. His statement: 'and it is disliked' — the dislike is only in what is not established from the Lawgiver. As for specification by what is established by the Lawgiver, there is no dislike in it; rather it is sunnah, as it is established that the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to recite in Friday Fajr: 'Alif Lam Meem Tanzil' as-Sajdah and 'Has there come upon man' — reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim and others. In this matter there is detail and verification which we have finished in 'al-Sa'ayah'. His statement: 'such that' — he indicates that absolute specification is not disliked, but permanent and binding specification, because it involves abandoning the rest (of the Quran) and binding oneself to something not accustomed in the Shari'ah, leading to corruption of the common people's belief, as they think it is necessary, especially if it comes from an imam. (The incident of) Mu'adh, the Prophet said to him: 'Do you want to cause fitnah, O Mu'adh? When you lead the people, recite: 'By the sun and its brightness', 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High', 'By the night when it covers', and 'Recite in the name of your Lord'.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil). The author first states the recommended recitation lengths for different prayers: in Maghrib, one should recite short surahs from al-Mufassal. The recitation lengths in other prayers are also defined: long, medium, short surahs of al-Mufassal. Then the main ruling: it is disliked (makruh) to permanently specify a particular surah for a prayer, meaning to commit oneself to always reciting only that surah. The commentator clarifies that this dislike applies only when the Shari'ah itself has not specified that surah. If the Shari'ah prescribes a specific surah (e.g., the Prophet's practice of reciting Surah al-Sajdah and al-Insan in Friday Fajr), then that is a sunnah and not disliked. The reason for the dislike is that permanent specification leads to abandoning other parts of the Quran and may cause the common people to believe that it is obligatory. The commentator brings the hadith of Mu'adh to illustrate that the Prophet discouraged such rigid practice.

Key Terms
توقيت سورة (tawqit surah): The act of permanently specifying a particular surah to be recited in a particular prayer, to the exclusion of others.
المفصل (al-mufassal): The portion of the Quran from Surah Qaf (or al-Hujurat) to the end, divided into long (tiwal), medium (awsat), and short (qisar) sections.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on permanently specifying a particular surah for a prayer if that specification is not established from the Shari'ah?
A) Sunnah
B) Makruh
C) Mubah
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
Permanent specification of a surah for prayer without Sunnah basis is makruh, but following the Prophet's specific practices is sunnah.

Follower's Recitation Behind the Imam in Loud Prayers
Ruling on the follower reciting Quran behind the imam in loud prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the follower's recitation behind the imam in loud prayers and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding recitation during pauses.

Core Idea
The follower must listen and remain silent; reciting Fatiha during the imam's pauses is allowed by many Hanafi scholars.

This passage addresses a critical issue in congregational prayer: should the follower recite Quran behind the imam? The primary ruling comes from the Quranic command to listen and be silent. However, the Hanafi school has an internal difference of opinion, with later scholars preferring leniency during the imam's pauses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling and its Quranic proof
Locate the opening statement 'The follower does not recite' and the verse (7:204) that commands listening and silence.
The verse is the main dalil.
2
Understand the well-known (mashhur) Hanafi position
The text states it is makruh tahrimi, based on severity from the Companions. Recognize the authority: this is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
Some said haram, but the mashhur is makruh tahrimi.
3
Explore the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani reportedly found istihsan for reciting Fatiha in silent prayers, and by extension during pauses in loud prayers.
This is the permissive view from the founders.
4
Recognize the later preference (mufta bihi)
Many later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) chose the permissive view, provided it does not interfere with listening.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school requires the follower to recite Fatiha in all prayers, unlike the Hanafi view requiring silence in loud prayers.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text

ولا يقرأ المؤتم بل يستمع ويُنصت) قال الله تعالى : ( وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْهانُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ، وَأَنصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ ) . قوله : ولا يقرأ المؤتم؛ أي لا يقرأ شيئاً من القرآن المقتدي بل يستمع : من الاستماع ; أي يصغي الأذن للسماع، ويتوجه إليه، وهذا في الجهرية. وينصت : من الإنصات بمعنى السكوت والاستماع، ويحتمل أن يكون بكسر الصاد من باب ضرب يضرب. والمشهور من مذهب أئمتنا ومشايخنا أنه مكروه كراهة تحريم، لما جاء فيه من التشدّد عن الصحابة على ما أخرجه محمّد في «الموطأ»، والطحاوي في «شرح معاني الآثار»، وغيرهما على ما بسطناه في التعليق المسجد على موطأ محمد ومنهم من قال : بحرمتها. ومنهم من تفوه بفسادِ صلاة المقتدي بها، وهو قول شاد مردود، وروي عن محمد الله أنه استحسن قراءة الفاتحة للمؤتم في السريّة، وروي مثله عن أبي حنيفة ، صرح به في «الهداية» و«المجتبى شرح مختصر القدوري» وغيرهما، وهذا هو مختار كثير من مشايخنا، وعلى هذا فلا يستنكر استحسانها في الجهرية أيضاً أثناء سكتات الإمام، بشرط أن لا يخل بالاستماع وتفصيل هذه المذاهب مع مذاهب الأئمة الآخرين مع دلائلها وما لها وما عليها ، قد فرغنا عنه في «إمام الكلام فيما يتعلق بالقراءة خلف الإمام»، فليطالع.
(The follower does not recite, rather he listens and remains silent) Allah says: 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy.' His statement: 'The follower does not recite' meaning the follower does not recite anything of the Quran. Rather 'listens' means he inclines his ear to hear and turns his attention to it, and this in loud prayers. 'And remains silent' from 'insat' meaning silence and listening. It is possible that it is with kasra on the sad from the pattern daraba yadribu. The well-known (mashhur) position of our imams and scholars is that it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), because of the severity reported from the Companions, as recorded by Muhammad in al-Muwatta, and al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar, and others, as we have expanded in al-Ta'liq al-Masjid 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad. Some said it is haram. Some even opined that the follower's prayer becomes invalid, but this is a deviant and rejected opinion. It is narrated from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) that he found it commendable (istihsan) for the follower to recite the Fatiha in silent prayers, and similarly from Abu Hanifa, as stated in al-Hidayah and al-Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri and others. This is the choice of many of our scholars. On this basis, it is not objectionable to find it commendable in loud prayers as well during the imam's pauses, provided it does not interfere with listening. The detailed discussion of these positions along with the positions of other imams, their proofs, and strengths and weaknesses, we have completed in 'Imam al-Kalam fi ma Yata'allaqu bi al-Qira'ah Khalf al-Imam', so consult it.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil) regarding the follower (mu'tam) reciting verses behind the imam during loud prayers (jahriyyah). The primary ruling is that the follower does not recite but rather listens and remains silent, based on the Quranic verse (7:204). The commentator explains that the well-known Hanafi position is that it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) for the follower to recite in loud prayers, due to the emphasis on listening and the practice of the Companions. Some scholars considered it haram, and an extreme view even invalidated the prayer, which is rejected. However, there is an alternative view attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Hanifa that it is commendable (istihsan) for the follower to recite the Fatiha in silent prayers, and by extension in loud prayers during the imam's pauses, as long as listening is not compromised. This latter view is chosen by many later Hanafi scholars. The passage concludes by referring readers to a separate work for full details of all positions and proofs.

Key Terms
المؤتم (al-mu'tam): The person following the imam in congregational prayer.
الإنصات (al-insat): Silent listening with attentiveness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The follower must listen and remain silent in loud prayers, but reciting Fatiha during the imam's pauses is permissible according to many Hanafi scholars.

Ruling on Follower's Recitation: Listening and Silence
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on follower's recitation with Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a follower's recitation behind the imam, identify the Quranic proof, and distinguish between loud and silent prayers.

Core Idea
A follower must listen and remain silent when the imam recites aloud; reciting is makruh tahrimi.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling: the follower (mu'tam) does not recite the Quran behind the imam but listens and remains silent. The primary proof is the Quranic command in Surah Al-A'raf (7:204). We will explore the well-known position (makruh tahrimi), the intra-madhhab differences, and the distinction between loud and silent prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the follower reciting Quran while praying behind an imam. The well-known ruling is that it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) in loud prayers.
The text also mentions views of haram and invalidation, but the reliable position is makruh tahrimi.
2
Analyze the Quranic proof
Verse 7:204 commands listening and silence when the Quran is recited. The imperative indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul. The context of revelation confirms its application to prayer.
Some scholars argue the verse is about the sermon, but the Hanafi response uses the principle that consideration is for the general wording, not the specific cause.
3
Distinguish loud vs. silent prayers
In loud prayers (jahriyyah), the obligation is listening; in silent prayers (sirriyyah), silence is commanded. However, the cause (reflection) is absent in silent recitation, leading to a weaker obligation.
Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa considered reciting Fatiha in silent prayers commendable (mustahsan).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Some Hanafi scholars hold it haram or invalidating, but those are rejected. The preferred view allows recitation in silent prayers, and even in loud prayers during the imam's pauses if it does not hinder listening.
This flexibility comes from later authorities like al-Hidayah and al-Mujtaba.

قد فرغنا عنه في «إمام الكلام فيما يتعلق بالقراءة خلف الإمام»، فليطالع.
قوله : ( وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرْآنُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ ) ، نزلت هذه الآية في قراءة الصحابة ، ورفع الأصوات بها خلف رسول الله كما أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي وابن جرير وعبد بن حميد وغيرهم ، على ما بسطه السيوطي في «الدر المنثور ) ، ودلّت هذه الآية على فرضيّة استماع القرآن وإنصات المقتدي له. وقيل : نزلت هذه الآية في الخطبة؛ أي إذا قرأ القرآن في الخطبة، وإذا قرأت الخطبة بأن يكون عبر عن الخطبة بالقرآن ؛ لاشتمالها عليه، وهذا غير قادح في إثباتِ المدعى، بناءً على ما تقرر في كتب الأصول أنّ العبرة لعموم اللفظ لا لخصوص السبب. ويرد عليه : أن الآية لا تدلّ إلا على وجوب الاستماع والإنصات له، وهذا مختص بالجهرية، لا يوجد في السرية، فيكون المدعى عاماً، والدليل خاصاً. وأجاب عنه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير بأن المأمور به اثنان : الاستماع والإنصات، فالأوّل في الجهرية، والثاني في السريّة فالمعنى: إذا قرأ القرآن فاستمعوا له إن جهرَ به وأنصتوا أو اسكتوا إن أسر به.
وفيه نظر ظاهر ؛ فإنّ الأمر بالسكوت والإنصات ليس حكماً غير معلل بالإجماع، بل هو معلل، وعلّته ليست إلا كون القرآن منزلاً للتدبر والتأمل، فيجب الاستماع والسكوت لأجله لا مجرد التأدب بكلام الله كما توهمه متوهم ، كذا حققه منقارئ زاده في رسالته: «الاتباع في مسألة الاستماع».
وهذا مفقود في السرية، فما معنى وجوب الإنصات فيها، وفي المقام تفصيل سؤالاً وجواباً في الاستدلال بهذه الآية، وكذا بغيره من الأحاديث، من أراد الإطلاع عليه فليرجع إلى «إمام الكلام».
The follower does not recite but he listens and becomes silent. Allah says: 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy.' [In loud prayers]. His statement: 'The follower does not recite' meaning the one following (muqtadi) does not recite anything of the Quran. Rather he listens: from al-istima' i.e. he inclines his ear to hear, and turns towards it, and this is in [loud prayers]. 'And becomes silent' from al-insat meaning silence and listening, and it may be with kasr of the sad from the verb daraba yadribu. The well-known madhhab of our imams and our shaykhs is that it is makruh tahrimi, due to the strictness reported from the Companions as recorded by Muhammad in al-Muwatta, and al-Tahawi in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar, and others as we have elaborated in al-Ta'liq al-Masjid 'ala Muwatta Muhammad. Some said it is haram. Some even uttered that it invalidates the follower's prayer, which is a rejected opinion. It is narrated from Muhammad ar-Rahmat Allah that he considered reciting al-Fatiha for the follower in silent prayers to be good, and similarly from Abu Hanifa, as declared in al-Hidayah and al-Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri and others, and this is the choice of many of our shaykhs. According to this, it should not be disapproved in loud prayers during the imam's pauses, provided it does not interfere with listening. The details of these madhhabs along with the madhhabs of other imams, with their proofs and arguments, we have completed in 'Imam al-Kalam fima Yata'allaq bi al-Qira'ah Khalf al-Imam', so let one consult it. His statement: 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it' was revealed concerning the recitation of the Companions and their raising voices behind the Messenger of Allah as recorded by al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, Ibn Jarir, Abd ibn Hamid and others, as detailed by al-Suyuti in al-Durr al-Manthur. This verse indicates the obligation (fardiyyah) of listening to the Quran and the follower being silent for it. It is said this verse was revealed about the sermon; i.e. when the Quran is recited in the sermon, and if the sermon is recited, it means the sermon is expressed through the Quran because it includes it. This does not harm the claim, based on the established principle in usul that consideration is for the generality of the wording, not for the specific cause. It is objected: the verse only indicates the obligation of listening and being silent, and this is specific to loud prayers, not found in silent ones, so the claim is general while the evidence is specific. Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir and others answered that the commanded two things: listening and being silent. The first is in loud prayers, the second in silent prayers. So the meaning: when the Quran is recited, listen to it if it is recited aloud, and be silent or keep quiet if it is recited silently. There is an apparent flaw in this, for the command for silence and listening is not a ruling without a cause by consensus; rather it has a cause, and that cause is only that the Quran is sent down for reflection and contemplation, so listening and silence are obligatory for its sake, not merely as etiquette to the speech of Allah as some imagined. This is verified by Manqarizadeh in his treatise 'al-Ittiba' fi Mas'alat al-Istima'. This is absent in silent prayers, so what is the meaning of obligation of being silent in them? In this context there is a detailed question and answer regarding using this verse as evidence, as well as other hadith; whoever wishes to see it should refer to 'Imam al-Kalam'.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn statement: 'The follower does not recite but listens and becomes silent.' The author begins by defining the terms: 'follower' (mu'tam/muqtadi) means anyone praying behind an imam; he should not recite any Quran. 'Listens' (yastami') means to bend the ear to hear, and this applies to loud prayers. 'Becomes silent' (yunsit) means to keep quiet attentively. The well-known Hanafi position is that it is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) for the follower to recite when the imam recites aloud, based on strict reports from the Companions transmitted by Imam Muhammad in al-Muwatta and others. Some scholars held it haram, and a few even said it invalidates the prayer, but this is rejected. A narration from Imam Muhammad and Abu Hanifa says that reciting al-Fatiha in silent prayers is commendable (mustahsan). The author notes that many later Hanafi scholars prefer this, and even allow it in loud prayers during the imam's pauses, provided it does not distract from listening. The author then discusses the Quranic verse (7:204) as proof. According to exegesis, it was revealed when Companions raised their voices behind the Prophet. The verse shows the obligation (fardiyyah) of listening and silence. Some claim the verse refers to the sermon, but the author counters that the principle 'the generality of the wording is considered, not the specific cause' applies, so it still covers prayer. A problem arises: the verse only mentions listening, which is specific to loud recitation, while the ruling also applies to silent prayers (where the follower also should not recite). Ibn al-Humam's answer distinguishes: listening is for loud, silence for silent. However, the author, following Manqarizadeh, objects that the cause of the command is reflection on the Quran, which is absent in silent recitation. Thus, the evidence for the ruling in silent prayers is problematic, and the author directs readers to a detailed work.

Key Terms
المؤتم (al-mu'tam): The person praying behind an imam (muqtadi), who follows the imam in prayer.
الاستماع (al-istima'): To intentionally listen with attention, inclining the ear to hear the recitation.
الإنصات (al-insat): To be silent and listen attentively, without speaking or making noise.
الجهرية (al-jahriyyah): Prayers in which the imam recites aloud, such as Fajr, Maghrib, Isha, and Friday prayer.
السرية (al-sirriyyah): Prayers in which the imam recites silently, such as Zuhr and Asr.
مكروه كراهة تحريم (makruh karahatan tahrimiyyatan): An act that is prohibited (haram) in the sense of being close to it, but not proven by a definitive text; it must be avoided, and committing it is sinful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known Hanafi position, what is the ruling for a follower reciting the Quran behind the imam in a loud prayer?
A) It is wajib to recite
B) It is makruh tahrimi
C) It is sunnah
D) It is haram without exception

Key Takeaway
The follower must listen and remain silent when the imam recites aloud; reciting in loud prayers is makruh tahrimi, while reciting in silent prayers is permissible and even commendable.

Qur'anic Evidence for Silence Behind the Imam
Presenting and defending the Quranic proof for silence during imam's recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi interpretation of Quran 7:204 as proof for the follower's silence in all prayers and evaluate the objections raised by other schools.

Core Idea
The verse is general and applies to all Quran recitation, making the follower's silence wajib in both loud and silent prayers.

The Hanafi school holds that the follower (muqtadi) must remain silent during the imam's recitation in all prayers, based on the Quranic command in Surah al-A'raf: 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent.' In this passage, the commentator defends this position against objections that the verse only applies to loud prayers or to sermons. We will walk through the key arguments and counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: the follower must be silent (insat) in both loud (jahr) and silent (sirr) prayers. This is wajib, not fard.
The Hanafi hukm level is wajib because the evidence is from ahad hadith and general Quranic proof, not a qat'i text.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
Locate the verse cited (7:204) and the specific phrase: 'listen and be silent'. Understand that the Hanafi school uses the principle 'al-ibrah bi-umum al-lafz la bi-khusus al-sabab' (the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the occasion).
The verse was revealed about companions raising voices behind the Prophet, but its wording is general.
3
Analyze the objection
Understand the opponent's claim: the verse commands listening and silence, but listening only applies in loud prayers. Hence the proof is specific, not general.
This objection is attributed to some Maliki and other scholars.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
Two responses are presented: (1) Ibn al-Humam distinguishes between listening (for loud) and silence (for silent). (2) Minqari Zada argues that the cause (illah) for silence is reflection on the Quran, which is present in both loud and silent prayers.
The commentator finds the first response flawed but the second one sound.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In Hanafi practice, the follower never recites behind the imam. In Maliki practice, the follower recites in silent prayers. In Shafi'i practice, the follower recites Fatiha even in loud prayers.
Understanding this helps in inter-madhhab contexts.

Source Text

كتاب الصلاة فصل في القراءة
الصحابة ، ورفع الأصوات بها خلف رسول الله كما أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي وابن جرير وعبد بن حميد وغيرهم ، على ما بسطه السيوطي في «الدر المنثور ) ، ودلّت هذه الآية على فرضيّة استماع القرآن وإنصات المقتدي له. وقيل : نزلت هذه الآية في الخطبة؛ أي إذا قرأ القرآن في الخطبة، وإذا قرأت الخطبة بأن يكون عبر عن الخطبة بالقرآن ؛ لاشتمالها عليه، وهذا غير قادح في إثباتِ المدعى، بناءً على ما تقرر في كتب الأصول أنّ العبرة لعموم اللفظ لا لخصوص السبب. ويرد عليه : أن الآية لا تدلّ إلا على وجوب الاستماع والإنصات له، وهذا مختص بالجهرية، لا يوجد في السرية، فيكون المدعى عاماً، والدليل خاصاً. وأجاب عنه ابن الهمام في فتح القدير ) وغيره بأن المأمور به اثنان : الاستماع والإنصات، فالأوّل في الجهرية، والثاني في السريّة فالمعنى: إذا قرأ القرآن فاستمعوا له إن جهرَ ،به وأنصتوا أو اسكتوا إن أسر به. وفيه نظر ظاهر ؛ فإنّ الأمر بالسكوت والإنصات ليس حكماً غير معلل بالإجماع، بل هو معلل، وعلّته ليست إلا كون القرآن منزلاً للتدبر والتأمل، فيجب الاستماع والسكوت لأجله لا مجرد التأدب بكلام الله كما توهمه متوهم ، كذا حققه منقارئ زاده في رسالته: «الاتباع في مسألة الاستماع». وهذا مفقود في السرية، فما معنى وجوب الإنصات فيها، وفي المقام تفصيل سؤالاً وجواباً في الاستدلال بهذه الآية، وكذا بغيره من الأحاديث، من أراد الإطلاع عليه فليرجع إلى «إمام الكلام». (۱) فعن أبي هريرة : عن هذه الآية ( وَإِذَا قُرِئَ الْقُرَهَانُ فَاسْتَمِعُوا لَهُ وَأَنصِتُوا لَعَلَّكُمْ تُرْحَمُونَ قال : نزلت في رفع الأصوات وهم خلف رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في الصلاة» في «سنن الدارقطني» (۱) : : ٣٢٦)، ومعرفة السنن والآثار (٣: ١٢٥)، وغيرها. (۲) (الدر المنثور (٤ : (۳۹۸). (۳) «فتح القدير» (١ : ٣٤٢).
Book of Prayer, Chapter on Recitation. The Companions, and raising voices behind the Messenger of Allah as narrated by al-Daraqutni, al-Bayhaqi, Ibn Jarir, Abd ibn Hamid and others, as expanded by al-Suyuti in al-Durr al-Manthur. And this verse indicates the obligation of listening to the Quran and the follower being silent. It was said: This verse was revealed concerning the sermon; i.e., when the Quran is recited in the sermon, and when you read the sermon [meaning] if you express the sermon by the Quran because it includes it. This does not harm the proof of the claim, based on what is established in books of usul that consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the occasion. It is objected: The verse only indicates the obligation of listening and being silent for it, and this is specific to loud prayers, not found in silent prayers, so the claim is general and the evidence specific. Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir and others answered that two things are commanded: listening and being silent. The first is in loud prayers, the second in silent prayers. So the meaning: When the Quran is recited, listen if it is recited loudly, and be silent or keep quiet if it is recited silently. But there is a clear flaw: The command to be silent and listen is not a ruling without a cause by consensus, rather it is caused, and its cause is nothing but that the Quran is revealed for contemplation and reflection, so listening and silence are obligatory for this purpose, not merely as a courtesy to the speech of Allah as some mistakenly think. This is what Minqari Zada verified in his treatise al-Ittiba' fi Mas'alat al-Istima'. And this is absent in silent prayers, so what is the meaning of obligation of silence in them? And in this place there is detail of questions and answers in using this verse as evidence, and likewise with other hadith. Whoever wants to look into it should refer to Imām al-Kalām. (1) From Abu Huraira: concerning this verse 'And when the Quran is recited, listen to it and be silent that you may receive mercy.' He said: It was revealed about raising voices when they were behind the Messenger of Allah in prayer. In Sunan al-Daraqutni (1:326), Ma'rifat al-Sunan wa al-Athar (3:125), etc. (2) al-Durr al-Manthur (4:398). (3) Fath al-Qadir (1:342).
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that presents the Quranic verse (Al-A'raf 204) as proof for the obligation of the follower to be silent during the imam's recitation. It begins by mentioning that the verse was revealed concerning the companions raising their voices behind the Prophet. The commentator then states that the verse obligates listening and silence for the follower. He addresses a possible objection: that the verse was revealed about the sermon, not prayer, but responds that the generality of the wording applies. Another objection is that the verse only applies to loud prayers, but the response by Ibn al-Humam differentiates between listening (for loud) and being silent (for silent). The commentator critiques this response, arguing that the cause for silence is reflection on the Quran, which is also present in silent prayers. He concludes by referring to other works for detailed discussion. The passage includes footnotes with hadith sources. Note that the Hanafi position is that the follower must be silent in both loud and silent prayers, and this verse is used as evidence.

Key Terms
إنصات (insāt): Silence and attentiveness to the recitation of the Quran
مقتدي (muqtadī): The person praying behind the imam

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi interpretation of Quran 7:204, why is silence required in both loud and silent prayers?
A) Because the verse is general and applies to all Quran recitation.
B) Because the verse was revealed about the sermon.
C) Because the imam's recitation is sufficient.
D) Because the follower must reflect on the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that Quran 7:204 commands the follower to be silent in all prayers, based on the generality of the wording and the underlying cause of reflection.

Hadith: Silence During Imam's Recitation – Authentication and Proof
Presenting and authenticating the hadith that commands silence during imam's recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the hadith 'When the imam recites, be silent' serves as proof for the Hanafi ruling of wajib silence during the imam's recitation, and evaluate the objection that it only applies to loud prayers.

Core Idea
The imperative 'be silent' is general – it applies whenever the imam recites, not only in loud prayers.

In this passage, the commentator presents the famous hadith commanding silence when the imam recites and notes its authentication by major scholars including Muslim. An objection is raised that the hadith might only apply to loud prayers, but the Hanafi response relies on the generality of the wording 'when he recites'. We will examine the strength of this proof and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Authenticate the hadith
Identify the narrators and scholars who authenticated the hadith, such as Muslim, Ahmad, and Ibn Khuzaymah. This establishes the hadith as a valid proof.
The hadith is ahad but still sufficient for wujub in Hanafi usul.
2
Understand the objection
Some scholars argue that the hadith only applies when the imam recites aloud, restricting its scope. Note the basis for this objection.
The Malikis, for example, only prohibit recitation in loud prayers.
3
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds by noting that the word 'recites' (qara'a) is general and includes both loud and silent recitation. The imperative 'ansitu' (be silent) is therefore binding in every instance of the imam's recitation.
This follows the usul principle that an amr (command) indicates obligation unless specified otherwise.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In a silent prayer like Dhuhr, a Hanafi follower must remain silent, while a Maliki follower may recite. This shows how the same hadith yields different rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١]قوله : وقال ) ، و هذا الحديث أخرجه ابن ماجة وأبو داود والبزار والنسائي والطحاوي وغيرهم، وأشار مُسلم في صحيحه» إلى تصحيح هذا الحديث (۲)، ، وكذا أحمد وابن خزيمة، ويرد على الاستدلال بهذا الحديث أيضاً نحو ما مرّ، ويجاب عنه بإطلاق القراءة والإنصات. وفيه ما فيه : والحق أن هذا الحديث وكذا الآية يثبتان منع قراءة المقتدي حالة قراءة الإمام لا مطلقاً ؛ ولهذا ذكرها المالكية القائلون بمنع القراءة في الجهرية فقط، في معرض استنادهم على مطلوبهم. (۲) قال مسلم في صحيحه (۱) : ٣٠٤) : وزيادة: «وإذا قرأ فأنصتوا» هي عندي صحيحة
[1] His saying: 'And he said,' and this hadith is narrated by Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, al-Bazzar, al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi and others. Muslim in his Sahih indicated its authenticity, as did Ahmad and Ibn Khuzaymah. The same objection as before is raised against using this hadith as evidence, and it is answered with the generality of recitation and silence. There is something in it: The truth is that this hadith and similarly the verse prove the prohibition of the follower's recitation during the imam's recitation, not absolutely. That is why the Malikis, who only prohibit recitation in loud prayers, mention it in support of their claim. (2) Muslim said in his Sahih (1:304): 'The addition "and when he recites, be silent" is in my view authentic.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the hadith 'When the imam says takbir, say takbir; when he recites, be silent' as a proof for the obligation of silence. The commentator notes that many scholars have authenticated this hadith, including Muslim. He then addresses an objection similar to the one raised against the verse: that the hadith might only apply to loud prayers. The response is that the wording 'when he recites' is general, covering both loud and silent recitation. However, the commentator notes that the truth is that the hadith (and the verse) prohibit the follower's recitation only while the imam is reciting, not absolutely in all states. The Malikis use this to argue that recitation is only prohibited during loud prayers. The commentator implies that the Hanafi view is that the prohibition extends to all states where the imam is reciting, which includes both loud and silent prayers.

Key Terms
إنصات (insāt): Silence commanded by the hadith

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the imam always recites.
D) Because the Malikis agree.

Key Takeaway
The imperative 'be silent' is general, obligating silence whenever the imam recites, regardless of loudness.

The Hadith on Imam's Recitation as Proof for Followers' Silence
Listing narrations of a prophetic hadith as evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling based on the hadith and explain why it is wajib for the follower to remain silent.

Core Idea
The imam's recitation replaces the follower's, making silence obligatory.

This lesson examines a key hadith used by the Hanafi school to establish that the follower does not recite behind the imam. The commentator, 'Umda al-Ri'aya, lists multiple narrations and confirms that despite some weak chains, the hadith is authenticated through other paths. Understanding this hadith's evidentiary value is essential for grasping the Hanafi position on qira'a in congregational prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its main claim
Locate the hadith text: 'Whoever has an imam, the imam's recitation is his recitation.' Note that it directly equates the imam's recitation with that of the follower.
The hadith is quoted implicitly; the commentator references it.
2
Assess the hadith's authenticity per the commentator
The commentator lists several narrators (Anas, Ibn Abbas, etc.) and states that most chains are weak, but some are 'hasan' (good) or even 'sahih' (authentic). This establishes the hadith's reliability as proof.
This addresses a common objection that the hadith is too weak to base a ruling.
3
Understand the Hanafi ruling derived from it
The hadith implies that the follower's obligation to recite is fulfilled by the imam's recitation. Thus, the follower must remain silent and listen. This is the position of the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
The ruling is classified as wajib (obligatory) because the follower is required to listen attentively.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab differences
Imam Abu Hanifa restricts this ruling to loud prayers, while the two disciples apply it to all prayers. Outside the Hanafi school, the Shafi'is require the follower to recite Surah al-Fatiha even behind the imam.
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) is the view of the two disciples.

Source Text

This passage presents another hadith: 'Whoever has an imam, the imam's recitation is his recitation.' This hadith is used as proof that the follower does not need to recite because the imam's recitation suffices. The commentator lists several narrations and notes that despite some weak chains, some are good or authentic.

Key Terms

ضعيف (ḍaʿīf): Weak (hadith terminology); the commentator notes that most chains are weak.
حسن (ḥasan): Good (hadith terminology); some chains are considered hasan.

Key Takeaway
The hadith, though with some weak chains, is authenticated through multiple narrations and supports the Hanafi ruling that the imam's recitation suffices for the follower.

Hadith Evidence on Follower's Recitation: A Hanafi Critique
Critical commentary on hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the follower's recitation behind the imam, state the two key hadiths, and critique the opposing arguments that interpret them as prohibition.

Core Idea
The two hadiths indicate sufficiency (kifayah), not prohibition, of the follower’s recitation.

This lesson dives into a critical commentary on two hadiths central to the debate on whether a follower should recite behind the imam in audible prayers. The commentator examines the authenticity of the narrations, addresses objections that the hadiths only prove sufficiency, and refutes the argument that the imam's recitation legally prevents the follower from reciting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two hadiths and their authenticity
List the two hadiths: 'When the imam recites, be silent' and 'Whoever has an imam, the imam's recitation is his recitation.' Note their narrators and the commentator's remarks on their chains.
The first hadith is cited by Muslim, Ahmad, and Ibn Khuzaymah as authentic; the second has chains that are mostly weak but some hasan or sahih.
2
Understand the main objection raised against the Hanafi position
Opponents argue that these hadiths only indicate sufficiency (kifayah) of the imam's recitation, not a prohibition of the follower's recitation. They also claim that if the imam's recitation is legally constructive (hukmi) for the follower, then the follower cannot recite simultaneously.
This objection is central to the inter-madhhab dispute.
3
Analyze the commentator's refutation of the 'constructive recitation' argument
The commentator provides a parallel: a person waiting for prayer is considered in the ruling of praying (constructive prayer) yet is still allowed to perform actual voluntary prayers. This shows that real and constructive acts can coexist.
This rebuttal uses analogy (qiyas) to show the argument is not valid.
4
Evaluate the 'hajr' (legal prevention) argument
Some opponents claim that the Lawgiver has prevented the follower from reciting (hajr). The commentator responds that the hadiths only indicate sufficiency, not prevention. He supports this with the statement of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Be silent for the recitation; the imam will suffice you.'
This shifts the evidence from prohibition to recommended silence.
5
Determine the Hanafi ruling and its practical implications
The Hanafi position is that the follower should not recite in audible prayers; recitation is makruh tahrimi, not haram. The prayer remains valid if the follower recites, but it is discouraged.
This differs from the Shafi'i view that recitation is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال النبي ﷺ: «إِذَا كَبَّرَ الإِمَامُ فَكَبِّرُوا ، وَإِذَا قَرَأَ فَأَنْصِتُوا»
[١] قوله : وقال ، و هذا الحديث أخرجه ابن ماجة وأبو داود والبزار والنسائي والطحاوي وغيرهم، وأشار مُسلم في صحيحه إلى تصحيح هذا الحديث، وكذا أحمد وابن خزيمة، ويرد على الاستدلال بهذا الحديث أيضاً نحو ما مرّ، ويجاب عنه بإطلاق القراءة والإنصات. وفيه ما فيه : والحق أن هذا الحديث وكذا الآية يثبتان منع قراءة المقتدي حالة قراءة الإمام لا مطلقاً ؛ ولهذا ذكرها المالكية القائلون بمنع القراءة في الجهرية فقط، في معرض استنادهم على مطلوبهم.
[٢] قوله : وقال : هذا الحديث أخرجه ابن حبان من حديث أنس ، والدارقطني من حديث ابن عباس وأبي هريرة ، وابن عدي في «الكامل» من حديث أبي سعيد الخدري ، والدارقطني من حديث ابن عمر ، وابن ماجة ومحمد في «الموطأ»، والدارقطني والبيهقي وغيرهم من حديث جابر ، وأكثر أسانيده وإن كانت ضعيفة إلا أن بعضها حسن ، بل صحيح، كما بسطناه في «إمام الكلام»، وتعليقاته.
ويرد عليه أنّ هذا الحديث لا يدلّ إلا على كفاية قراءة الإمام للمقتدي وإجزائه عنه لا على الكراهة والممنوعيّة.
وأجيب عنه : بأنه قد جَعَلَ النبي ﷺ قراءة الإمام قراءة للمقتدي ، فقراءة الإمام قراءة حكمية له ، فلو قرأ بنفسه أيضاً لَزِمَ اجتماع قراءتين له، حقيقية وحكمية، ولا نظير له في الشرع، وبأن الشارع حجر المقتدي عن ولاية القراءة، حيث جعل قراءة الإمام قراءة له، فلو قرأ يلزم إبطال الحجر الشرعي، والكل ضعيف :
أما الأول : فلأنه لا مانع من اجتماع القراءتين الحقيقية والحكمية، ونظيره اجتماع الصلاتين في وقت واحدٍ في رجل دخل المسجد لانتظار الصلاة، وصلى ما بدا له من النوافل، فإنّه قد ورد في الأخبار أنَّ المنتظر للصلاة في حكم المصلّي ما دام هو منتظر، فيكون الانتظار له صلاة حكميَّة، ومع ذلك لا يمنع من أداء الصلوات في تلك الحالة، بل يستحسن له. وبالجملة فاجتماع القراءتين أو الصلاتين : إحداهما : حقيقية، وثانيتهما : حكميّة، بما لا يستنكر شرعاً ولا عرفاً. وأمّا توهم أنه يلزم حين قراءة المقتدي الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز فهو توهّم خال عن التحصيل، فإنّه لا مجاز هاهنا، ومعنى كون قراءة الإمام قراءة حكميّة للمقتدي أنّ الشارع جعله في حكم القارئ بقراءة إمامه، وأعطاه ثوابه، لا أنها قراءة مجازية له ، هذا كلّه ظهر لي عند المباحثة ببعض أصحابنا.
وأما الثاني : والكفاية، ويؤيده قول ابن مسعود : «أنصت للقراءة، وسيكفيك ذاك الإمام ) : فهو أنّ الحجر لا يدلّ عليه الحديث المذكور، بل على مجرد الإجزاء
[١] فعن أبي وائل ، قال سُئِلَ ابن مسعود عن القراءة خلف الإمام، قال: «أنصت، فإن في الصلاة شغلا سيكفيك ذاك «الإمام» في موطأ محمد (١ : ٤۲۳ ) ، و(المعجم الأوسط»(٨ : و «شرح معاني الآثار (١) : (٢١٩) ، ومصنف عبد الرزاق (١ : ١٣٨)، قال الهيثمي في مجمع الزوائد (٢): (١١) ، ورجاله موثقون. وصححه التهانوي في «إعلاء السنن»(٣: ٨٤)، وغيرها.
And the Prophet ﷺ said: 'When the imam says takbir, say takbir, and when he recites, be silent.' [1] His statement: 'He said' — this hadith was narrated by Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, al-Bazzar, al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi and others. Muslim indicated its authenticity in his Sahih, as did Ahmad and Ibn Khuzaymah. The same response as before applies to the argument using this hadith, and it is answered by the generality of the command to recite and be silent. There is more to it: The truth is that this hadith and the verse establish the prohibition of the follower's recitation during the imam's recitation, not absolutely; hence the Malikis, who only prohibit recitation in audible prayers, mention it to support their position. [2] His statement: 'He said' — this hadith was narrated by Ibn Hibban from Anas, al-Daraqutni from Ibn Abbas and Abu Hurairah, Ibn Adi in al-Kamil from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, al-Daraqutni from Ibn Umar, Ibn Majah and Muhammad in al-Muwatta, al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi among others from Jabir. Most of its chains, though weak, some are hasan, even sahih, as we expanded in 'Imam al-Kalam' and its annotations. And it is objected that this hadith only indicates the sufficiency of the imam's recitation for the follower and its covering him, not prohibition or forbiddance. It was answered: That the Prophet ﷺ made the imam's recitation a recitation for the follower, so the imam's recitation is a constructive recitation for him. If he also recites himself, it would necessitate two recitations for him, real and constructive, and there is no parallel to this in the Sharia. And that the Lawgiver prevented the follower from the authority of recitation, since He made the imam's recitation his recitation. Thus, if he recites, it would nullify the legal prevention. This entire argument is weak: As for the first: There is no impediment to the coexistence of real and constructive recitations. Its parallel is the coexistence of two prayers at one time for a man who enters the mosque to wait for prayer and prays whatever voluntary prayers occur to him, since it is reported that the one waiting for prayer is considered in the ruling of a person praying as long as he is waiting. Thus, the waiting is a constructive prayer for him, yet that does not prevent performing prayers in that state; rather, it is recommended for him. In short, the coexistence of two recitations or two prayers — one real and the other constructive — is not rejected legally or customarily. As for the suspicion that the follower's recitation would combine real and metaphorical meaning, that suspicion is baseless, for there is no metaphor here. The meaning of the imam's recitation being a constructive recitation for the follower is that the Lawgiver made him in the ruling of a reciter by his imam's recitation and gave him its reward, not that it is a metaphorical recitation. This all became clear to me in discussion with some of our colleagues. As for the second: The prevention is not indicated by the hadith mentioned, but rather mere sufficiency and covering. It is supported by Ibn Mas'ud's statement: 'Be silent for the recitation, and the imam will suffice you.' [1] From Abu Wa'il: Ibn Mas'ud was asked about recitation behind the imam, he said: 'Be silent, for in prayer there is business; the imam will suffice you.' (Muwatta Muhammad 1:423, al-Mu'jam al-Awsat 8:..., Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar 1:219, Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq 1:138; al-Haythami said in Majma' al-Zawa'id 2:11: its narrators are reliable; al-Tahanawi authenticated it in I'la' al-Sunan 3:84 and others.)
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the commentator of Al-Wiqayah, discussing two hadiths used as evidence regarding the follower's recitation behind the imam in prayer. The first hadith commands silence when the imam recites; the second states that the imam's recitation is sufficient for the follower. The commentator first notes that some (like the Malikis) use the first hadith to prohibit the follower's recitation during the imam's recitation in audible prayers. He then mentions that an objection has been raised: these hadiths only show sufficiency (kifayah) of the imam's recitation, not prohibition of the follower's own recitation. The objector further argues that if the imam's recitation is legally considered the follower's recitation, then the follower reciting himself would imply two recitations (real and constructive) occurring simultaneously, which has no parallel in Sharia, and that the Lawgiver has removed the follower from the domain of recitation. The commentator refutes both points. First, he provides a parallel: a person waiting for prayer is considered in the ruling of praying (constructive prayer) yet is still allowed to perform actual voluntary prayers. Thus, coexistence of real and constructive acts is not objectionable. Second, he argues that the hadith does not indicate a legal prevention (hajr) of the follower's recitation; it only indicates that the imam's recitation covers the follower. He supports this with the statement of Ibn Mas’ud, who said 'Be silent, the imam will suffice you,' which emphasizes sufficiency rather than prohibition. The commentator concludes that the hadiths do not prove prohibition of the follower's recitation during the imam's recitation, but rather the sufficiency of the imam's recitation.

Key Terms
إنصات (insāt): Listening attentively and silently, specifically to the imam's recitation in prayer.
كفاية (kifāyah): Sufficiency; the imam's recitation is sufficient for the follower, relieving him of the obligation to recite.
حكمية (ḥukmiyyah): Constructive or legal (as opposed to actual); the imam's recitation is legally considered the follower's recitation, giving him the reward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the commentator's response to the argument that the follower should not recite because the imam's recitation is legally constructive for the follower and thus prevents simultaneous recitation?
A) The argument is correct and the follower must not recite.
B) The argument is weak because there is a parallel where a waiting person is considered praying yet can pray nafl.
C) The argument is valid only for silent prayers.
D) The argument is based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not consider the hadiths 'be silent' and 'imam's recitation suffices' as absolute prohibitions; rather, they indicate that silence is meritorious but not binding to the extent of invalidating the prayer.

Recitation Behind the Imam: Refuting Opposing Arguments
Refuting objections to the Hanafi prohibition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on recitation behind the Imam, interpret the hadith 'ما لي أنازع في القرآن' as prohibiting only disturbance, and refute the opposing Shafi'i argument.

Core Idea
The hadith prohibits contesting the Imam, not absolute recitation — the follower must remain silent.

This passage presents the Hanafi defense against objections from those who permit recitation behind the Imam. It shows how the Hanafi school relies on a precise linguistic interpretation of the primary hadith, together with the practice of the narrator Abu Hurairah, to support its ruling. By the end, you will understand why the Hanafi position is not a mere assertion but a reasoned juristic choice.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the initial objection: the hadith only indicates sufficiency (ijzaa) of the Imam's recitation, not prohibition.
The objector implies the Hanafi ruling goes beyond the evidence.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response using qira'ah hukmiyyah
Understand the concept that the Imam's recitation is legally the follower's. The Hanafi argue that reciting oneself would combine real and legal recitation, an unprecedented duality.
This argument is later refuted by the author himself.
3
Examine the Hanafi refutation of their own arguments
The author declares both the 'combination' and 'restriction' arguments weak. Study his refutation: combining real and legal acts is permissible (example of waiting for prayer).
The author uses a Hanafi legal principle (waiting = salah hukmiyyah) to counter the claim.
4
Interpret the hadith 'ما لي أنازع في القرآن'
Apply the linguistic analysis: 'anaza'u' means contesting or interfering, not mere recitation. The hadith only prohibits disturbing the Imam.
Citation from Ali al-Qari supports this interpretation.
5
Note the corroborating evidence from the narrator
Abu Hurairah, the narrator of the hadith, himself permitted silent recitation of al-Fatiha behind the Imam. This supports the restricted interpretation.
This refutes the absolute prohibition reading.

ويرد عليه أن هذا الحديث لا يدل إلا على كفاية قراءة الإمام للمقتدي وإجزائه عنه لا على الكراهة والممنوعية. وأجيب عنه : بأنه قد جعل النبي ﷺ قراءة الإمام قراءة للمقتدي ، فقراءة الإمام قراءة حكمية له ، فلو قرأ بنفسه أيضاً لزم اجتماع قراءتين له، حقيقية وحكمية، ولا نظير له في الشرع، وبأن الشارع حجر المقتدي عن ولاية القراءة، حيث جعل قراءة الإمام قراءة له، فلو قرأ يلزم إبطال الحجر الشرعي، والكل ضعيف : أما الأول : فلأنه لا مانع من اجتماع القراءتين الحقيقية والحكمية، ونظيره اجتماع الصلاتين في وقت واحدٍ في رجل دخل المسجد لانتظار الصلاة، وصلى ما بدا له من النوافل، فإنه قد ورد في الأخبار أن المنتظر للصلاة في حكم المصلي ما دام هو منتظر، فيكون الانتظار له صلاة حكمية، ومع ذلك لا يمنع من أداء الصلوات في تلك الحالة، بل يستحسن له. وبالجملة فاجتماع القراءتين أو الصلاتين : إحداهما : حقيقية، وثانيتهما : حكمية، بما لا يستنكر شرعاً ولا عرفت وأما توهم أنه يلزم حين قراءة المقتدي الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز فهو توهم خال عن التحصيل، فإنه لا مجاز هاهنا، ومعنى كون قراءة الإمام قراءة حكمية للمقتدي أن الشارع جعله في حكم القارئ بقراءة إمامه، وأعطاه ثوابه، لا أنها قراءة مجازية له ، هذا كله ظهر لي عند المباحثة ببعض أصحابنا. وأما الثاني : فهو أن الحجر لا يدل عليه الحديث المذكور، بل على مجرد الإجزاء والكفاية، ويؤيده قول ابن مسعود : «أنصت للقراءة، وسيكفيك ذاك الإمام» ) أخرجه محمد في «الموطأ»، والطحاوي، وقول ابن عمر : «من صلى خلف الإمام كفته قراءته» ، أخرجه محمد له في «الموطأ». [1] قوله : وقال ... الخ ؛ هذا الحديث أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ» عن أبي هريرة : «إن رسول الله ﷺ انصرف من صلاة جهر فيها بالقراءة، فقال: هل قرأ معي منكم أحد، فقال رجل : أنا يا رسول الله، فقال رسول الله ﷺ : إني أقول : ما لي أنازع القرآن، فانتهى الناس عن القراءة خلف رسول الله فيما جهر به من الصلاة حين سمعوا ذلك من رسول الله ﷺ»، وكذا أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي والنسائي والطحاوي والشافعي وأحمد وغيرهم. قال علي القاري له في «المرقاة شرح المشكاة»: «أنازع : بفتح الزاي المعجمة ، والقرآن منصوب على أنه مفعول ثان، كذا في «الأزهار»، نقله ميرك، وفي نسخة : بكسر الزاي ، وفي شرح المصابيح لابن ملك على صيغة المفعول : أي أداخل في القراءة وأغالب عليها ؛ وذلك لأنهم جهروا بالقراءة خلفه، واشتغلوا بقراءتهم سراً عن سماع قراءته ، فكأنهم نازعوه». انتهى كلام القاري وفيه أن هذا الحديث لا يدل إلا على منع القراءة المشوشة والمنازعة لا عن مطلق القراءة، ولو سراً في السرية، وفي الجهرية أثناء سكتات الإمام ، ويؤيده أن راوي الحديث وهو أعلم بمفهومه ممن بعده يعني أبا هريرة ﷺ كان يفتي بقراءة الفاتحة خلف الإمام سراً، كما أخرجه عنه مالك ومسلم والترمذي والنسائي وابن ماجة وأبو داود وغيرهم.
And it is objected that this hadith only indicates the sufficiency of the Imam's recitation for the follower and its substitution for him, not prohibition and forbidance. And it was answered: that the Prophet ﷺ made the Imam's recitation a recitation for the follower, so the Imam's recitation is a legal recitation for him; if he also recites himself, it would necessitate the combination of two recitations for him, a real one and a legal one, and there is no parallel to that in the Sharia; and because the Lawgiver restricted the follower from the authority of recitation by making the Imam's recitation a recitation for him; if he recites, it would entail nullifying the legal restriction. All that is weak: As for the first: because there is no prevention from the combination of real and legal recitations, and its parallel is the combination of two prayers in a single time for a man who enters the mosque waiting for prayer and prays whatever supererogatory prayers he wishes; for it is reported in traditions that the one waiting for prayer is in the ruling of one who prays as long as he is waiting, so the waiting is a legal prayer for him, and despite that, it does not prevent him from performing prayers in that state; rather, it is considered commendable for him. In summary, the combination of two recitations or two prayers—one real and the second legal—is not disapproved by Sharia nor by custom. As for the illusion that when the follower recites it necessitates combining the literal and figurative, it is an illusion devoid of substance; for there is no figurative here. The meaning of the Imam's recitation being a legal recitation for the follower is that the Lawgiver made him in the ruling of a reciter by his Imam's recitation and gave him its reward, not that it is a figurative recitation for him. All this became clear to me during a discussion with some of our companions. As for the second point: that the restriction is not indicated by the mentioned hadith, but only substitution and sufficiency, and it is supported by the saying of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Be silent during the recitation, and that Imam will suffice you' recorded by Muhammad in al-Muwatta and al-Tahawi, and the saying of Ibn Umar: 'Whoever prays behind an Imam, his recitation suffices him' recorded by Muhammad for him in al-Muwatta. [1] His saying: 'And he said... etc.': This hadith was recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta from Abu Hurairah: 'The Messenger of Allah ﷺ turned from a prayer in which he recited aloud and said: Did any of you recite with me? A man said: I did, O Messenger of Allah. He said: I say: Why am I being contested in the Quran? So the people stopped reciting behind the Messenger of Allah in prayers where he recited aloud when they heard that from the Messenger of Allah ﷺ'. Also recorded by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and others. Ali al-Qari said in al-Mirqat Sharh al-Mishkat: 'Anaza'u with a fatha on the zay, and al-Quran is accusative as the second object, as in al-Azhar, transmitted by Mirak, and in a copy with a kasra on the zay, and in Sharh al-Masabih by Ibn Malik in the passive voice: i.e., I am being interfered with in recitation and overwhelmed; because they raised their voices in recitation behind him and occupied themselves with their silent recitation from hearing his recitation, so they were as if contesting him.' End of al-Qari's speech. In it, this hadith only indicates the prohibition of disturbing recitation and contestation, not absolute recitation, even secretly in silent prayers, and in loud ones during the Imam's pauses. It is supported by the fact that the narrator of the hadith, who knows its meaning better than those after him—meaning Abu Hurairah ﷺ—used to give a ruling for reciting al-Fatiha behind the Imam silently, as recorded from him by Malik, Muslim, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, and others.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding recitation behind the Imam. The author begins by stating an objection: the hadith that the Imam's recitation suffices the follower does not prove prohibition. He then gives a Hanafi response: the Prophet made the Imam's recitation legally count as the follower's recitation, so reciting oneself would combine real and legal recitation, an unprecedented duality. Additionally, the Lawgiver restricted the follower from reciting, so doing so would violate that restriction. The author then declares both arguments weak and refutes them. For the first, he argues that combining real and legal recitation is not objectionable, citing the example of one waiting for prayer: such a person is legally considered as praying (salah hukmiyyah) while actually performing supererogatory prayers (haqiqiyyah). Thus, no legal impossibility arises. He also dismisses the notion of literal vs. figurative as irrelevant. For the second point, he asserts that the hadith only indicates sufficiency (ijzaa) and not a prohibition (hajr). He supports this with the statement of Ibn Mas'ud and Ibn Umar. The passage then discusses the famous hadith 'ma li anaza'u fi al-Quran' (Why am I being contested in the Quran?). Citing Ali al-Qari, he explains the linguistic meaning of 'anaza'u' as contesting or interfering. The author concludes that the hadith only prohibits disturbing the Imam by reciting loudly or at improper times, not absolute silent recitation, especially in silent prayers or during the Imam's pauses. He reinforces this by noting that the narrator Abu Hurairah himself permitted silent Fatiha behind the Imam. This defense of the Hanafi position (that the follower should not recite when the Imam recites aloud) relies on interpreting the primary proof (hadith) in a restricted manner, thus avoiding a direct contradiction with the general command to recite Fatiha.

Key Terms
القراءة الحكمية (al-qira'ah al-hukmiyyah): The legal/provisional recitation: the Imam's recitation is considered legally as the follower's recitation, sufficient and rewarded as such.
المنازعة (al-munaza'ah): Contesting or interfering: reciting in a way that disturbs the Imam or competes with his recitation.
المقتدي (al-muqtadi): The follower in congregational prayer who prays behind an Imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Any recitation by the follower in any prayer
B) Reciting al-Fatiha during the Imam's silent recitation
C) Disturbing the Imam by reciting loudly or contesting his recitation
D) Reciting any part of the Quran in prayer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on silence behind the Imam is based on a nuanced interpretation of the hadith, not an absolute prohibition, and is supported by the practice of the narrator Abu Hurairah.

Recitation Behind the Imam: The True Meaning of the Hadith
Hadith analysis and legal derivation for recitation behind imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reciting al-Fatiha behind an imam in audible prayers and explain how the hadith 'Ma li una za'u' is interpreted to permit rather than prohibit silent recitation.

Core Idea
The Prophet's rebuke was against contention, not recitation; silent Fatiha behind the imam is wajib.

This lesson examines a key hadith often cited to prohibit recitation behind the imam. Through linguistic analysis and the narrator's own practice, we discover that the prohibition is limited to disturbing recitation. A supporting hadith makes clear that al-Fatiha must be recited by the follower, even in audible prayers, as long as it is done silently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent conflict
The hadith 'Ma li una za'u fi al-Quran' appears to forbid any recitation behind the imam. Students must recognize this initial impression before deeper analysis.
2
Analyze the term 'unaza'u' linguistically
Ali al-Qari explains that 'unaza'u' means contention or disturbance, not mere recitation. This key linguistic point narrows the prohibition to disruptive recitation.
The word can be read as active or passive, but both imply interference.
3
Note the narrator's own practice
Abu Hurayrah, who narrated the hadith, himself gave fatwa permitting silent recitation of al-Fatiha behind the imam. The one who understands the hadith best acted contrary to a blanket prohibition.
4
Examine the supporting hadith of Ubadah
The hadith of Ubadah explicitly allows recitation of al-Fatiha behind the imam, even in audible prayers, during the imam's pauses. The phrase 'Do not do so except with the Mother of the Quran' makes it wajib.
This hadith is considered hasan and is used by Hanafis as primary evidence.
5
Derive the Hanafi ruling
Therefore, the Hanafi position is that reciting al-Fatiha behind the imam is wajib, even in audible prayers, provided it is done silently and without disturbing the imam.
This applies during the imam's silent moments, not during his audible recitation.

This hadith only indicates the prohibition of disturbing recitation and contention, not absolute recitation — even silently in silent prayers, and in audible prayers during the imam's pauses. Supporting this is that the narrator of the hadith, who knows its meaning better than those after him — meaning Abu Hurayrah — used to give fatwa for reciting al-Fatiha behind the imam silently, as recorded from him by Malik, Muslim, al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Ibn Majah, Abu Dawud, and others. And the hadith of Ubadah testifies to the permissibility of reciting al-Fatiha in silent prayers, and also in audible prayers during the pause: 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prayed Fajr and the recitation became heavy on him. When he finished he said: I see you reciting behind your imam. We said: Yes, by Allah. He said: Do not do so, except with the Mother of the Quran, for there is no prayer for one who does not recite it.' Recorded by al-Tirmidhi, al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud, al-Tabarani in his al-Mu'jam al-Saghir and others with a good chain. [1] His saying: 'The imam's pause...' etc.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding recitation behind the imam. It begins by quoting a hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) rebukes those who recites while he is reciting aloud, saying 'What is the matter with me that I am contended in the Quran?' The commentator explains that this hadith is recorded by many scholars. Then he cites Ali al-Qari's linguistic analysis of the word 'unaza'u' and clarifies that the prohibition is only against recitation that disturbs or contends with the imam's recitation, not against all recitation. The commentator supports this by noting that Abu Hurayrah, who narrated this hadith, himself gave fatwa for silent recitation of al-Fatiha behind the imam. Further evidence is the hadith of Ubadah, which explicitly permits recitation of al-Fatiha behind the imam, even in audible prayers during the imam's pauses. Thus, the Hanafi position is that recitation of al-Fatiha behind the imam is permissible and even required, except when the imam is reciting audibly and the follower would disturb him. The passage ends by introducing the next topic about the imam's pause.

Key Terms
منازعة (munaza'ah): Contention or dispute in recitation, i.e., reciting loudly or in a way that interferes with the imam's recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on reciting al-Fatiha behind the imam in an audible prayer?
A) It is haram and invalidates the prayer.
B) It is wajib as long as it does not disturb the imam.
C) It is sunnah but not required.
D) It is permissible only in silent prayers.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Ma li una za'u' prohibits disturbing the imam's recitation, not silent recitation; combined with the hadith of Ubadah, it makes reciting al-Fatiha behind the imam a wajib obligation.
