A Qari Cannot Follow an Ummi in Prayer: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Commentary on ruling that a reciter cannot follow a non-reciter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a qari cannot follow an ummi in prayer, explain the evidence from the hadith 'al-imamu damin' and the qiyas, and identify the intra-Hanafi differences on the definition of ummi.

Core Idea
A qari cannot follow an ummi: the strong cannot build upon the weak, because the imam's prayer incorporates the follower's.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: a person who can recite the Quran (qari) may not follow an imam who cannot (ummi). The ruling is based on the hadith 'The imam is a guarantor' and a clear qiyas. The passage also addresses differences within the Hanafi school on the definition of 'ummi' and a specific scenario involving proximity in the mosque.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Hukm Level
The ruling is that a qari should not follow an ummi in prayer. According to the Hanafi school, this is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). The reason is that the qari is stronger in recitation, and the strong cannot follow the weak.
2
Define Key Terms: Qari and Ummi
A qari is one who can recite enough Quran for prayer. An ummi is one who does not know enough. Abu Hanifa defines ummi as one who knows less than one verse; the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) define it as less than three short verses. The term 'ummi' implies being as unlettered as at birth.
This definitional difference affects when the ruling applies.
3
Understand the Principal Proof
The hadith 'al-imamu damin' (the imam is a guarantor) indicates that the imam's prayer incorporates the follower's. Therefore, the follower cannot be stronger than the imam. This leads to the principle: the strong cannot build upon the weak. Thus, a qari following an ummi is prohibited.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi with a sound chain.
4
Note Exceptions and Intra-Hanafi Disputes
If the qari is far from the ummi (e.g., at the mosque door) or praying a different prayer, the ummi may pray alone validly. However, if both are in the same mosque in separate corners and praying concurrently, there is a dispute: on analogy with Abu Hanifa's view, the ummi's prayer is invalid; a narration says it is valid.
This shows the school's internal flexibility.
5
Apply the Ruling to a Scenario
Example: A qari arrives late and finds the only imam is an ummi who knows one verse. According to the Hanafi position, the qari should not join that congregation; he should pray alone or wait for another congregation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل في هذه المسألة وما يماثلها حديث: «الإمام» ضامن ) ، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي بسند صحيح؛ فإنّه يدلّ على أنّ صلاة الإمام يتضمن صلاة المقتدي ، ومن المعلوم أن الشيء لا يتضمن ما هو فوقه وإنما يتضمن ما هو مثله أو دونه. ومن هاهنا أصل أصحابنا عدم جواز بناء القوي على الضعيف، وفرعوا عليه فروعاً كثيرة، وأجابوا عن أخبار دلت على خلافه في صور جزئية بأجوبة متعددة ، وموضع بسطها هو «السعاية». [١]قوله : وقارئ بأمي ؛ المراد بالأمي على ما ذكره تاج الشريعة في «شرح الهداية» وغيره : مَن لا يحفظ من القرآن قدر ما تجوز به صلاته، وهو آية عنده، وثلاث آيات قصار عندهما، والمراد بالقارئ من هو بخلافه»، وإنّما قيل له : الأمي؛ لكونه كما ولدته أمه في الجهل وعدم الحفظ. والوجه في ذلك : أن الأمي عاجز عن القراءة التي هي أحد أركان الصلاة، والقارئ قادر عليه، فإذا ترك فرض القراءة مع القدرة عليه بطلت صلاته. وذكر في «المحيط»: «إن كان القارئ على باب المسجد أو بجوار المسجد، والأمي في المسجد يصلّي وحده، فإنّ صلاته جائزة بالاتفاق، وكذا إذا كان القارئ في صلاة غير صلاة الأمي جاز للأمي أن يصلي وحده ولا ينتظر فراغ القارئ. أما إذا كان القارئ في ناحية المسجدِ والأمي في ناحية أخرى وصلاتهما متوافقة، فقد ذكر القاضي أبو خازم : إنّ على قياس قول أبي حنيفة له لا تجوز صلاة الأمي، وهو قول مالك ، وفي رواية : تجوز».
The basis for this issue and the like is the hadith: 'The imam is a guarantor,' narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi with a sound chain. For it indicates that the prayer of the imam incorporates the prayer of the follower, and it is known that a thing does not incorporate what is above it, but only what is like it or below it. From here our scholars derived the impermissibility of building the strong upon the weak, and they derived many subsidiary rulings from it, and they answered the reports that indicate otherwise in particular cases with multiple answers, and the place for their elaboration is al-Si'ayah. [1] His saying: 'And a reciter with an ummi': The meaning of 'ummi' according to Taj al-Shari'ah in Sharh al-Hidayah and others is one who does not memorize from the Quran enough to make his prayer valid—and that is one verse according to Abu Hanifa, and three short verses according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). The 'reciter' is the opposite. He is called 'ummi' because, as if he were as his mother bore him, in ignorance and lack of memorization. The reason for that is that the ummi is incapable of recitation which is one of the pillars of prayer, while the reciter is capable of it. So if he abandons the obligatory recitation while being able, his prayer becomes invalid. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit: 'If the reciter is at the door of the mosque or adjacent to the mosque, and the ummi is in the mosque praying alone, then his prayer is valid by agreement. Similarly, if the reciter is in a prayer other than the ummi's prayer, it is permissible for the ummi to pray alone and not wait for the reciter to finish. However, if the reciter is in one corner of the mosque and the ummi is in another corner and their prayers are concurrent, then Qadi Abu Khazim mentioned: On the analogy of Abu Hanifa's statement, the prayer of the ummi is not permissible, and that is the saying of Malik; and in one narration: it is permissible.'
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage lays out the general principle that the strong cannot follow the weak, derived from the hadith 'The imam is a guarantor.' The imam's prayer incorporates the follower's, so the follower must be equal or lesser in strength. The specific ruling discussed is that a reciter (qari') cannot follow an ummi, defined as one who does not know enough Quran for prayer. The reciter is capable of recitation, which is a pillar of prayer, while the ummi is incapable. Therefore, if the reciter were to follow the ummi, he would be abandoning an obligatory pillar while able, invalidating his prayer. However, there are exceptions when the reciter is far away (at the door) or praying a different prayer—then the ummi may pray alone. There is also a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars regarding a scenario where both are in the same mosque but separate corners.

Key Terms
أمي (ummi): A person who does not memorize enough Quran to make his prayer valid; according to Abu Hanifa: less than one verse; according to the two companions: less than three short verses.
قارئ (qari'): One who can recite the required amount of Quran in prayer, i.e., the opposite of ummi.
الإمام ضامن (al-imamu damin): The hadith indicating that the imam's prayer guarantees or incorporates the follower's prayer, establishing the principle that the follower cannot be stronger than the imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One who cannot recite any Quran
B) One who does not know one verse
C) One who does not know three short verses
D) One who is illiterate

Key Takeaway
A qari cannot follow an ummi in prayer because the imam's prayer guarantees the follower's and the strong cannot build upon the weak; this ruling is derived from hadith and qiyas, with intra-Hanafi differences on the precise definition of ummi.

A Clothed Person Cannot Follow a Naked Person in Prayer
Commentary on the ruling of a clothed person following a naked one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a clothed person may not follow a naked person in prayer and explain the legal reasoning (qiyas) behind it.

Core Idea
A clothed person cannot follow a naked one because the stronger in fulfilling a condition cannot follow the weaker.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in congregational prayer: a person who has proper clothing covering the awrah cannot follow an imam who lacks such covering. The reasoning is based on the principle that the one who fulfills a condition of prayer is in a higher state, and the higher cannot follow the lower. We will explore the definition of 'naked' in this context, the underlying condition of covering the awrah, and the qiyas (analogy) that supports the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a clothed person intending to follow a naked person as imam in congregational prayer. The ruling is that this is impermissible (makruh tahrimi).
The ruling applies to both obligatory and optional prayers.
2
Define the key terms
Understand what 'naked' (aari) and 'clothed' (labis) mean in this context. The 'naked' person is one who does not have sufficient clothing to cover the awrah for prayer. The 'clothed' person has such covering.
In Hanafi fiqh, the awrah for a man is from navel to knee; for a woman, the entire body except face and hands.
3
Identify the underlying condition
Covering the awrah is a condition (shart) for the validity of prayer. The naked person is missing this condition, while the clothed person fulfills it.
Conditions (shurut) are external requirements; if missing, the prayer is invalid.
4
Apply the qiyas (analogy)
The ruling is derived by analogy: just as a person who is pure cannot follow one who is impure, a person who fulfills a condition cannot follow one who lacks it. The clothed person is in a higher state (a'la) and cannot follow the lower state (adna).
This is qiyas jaliy (clear analogy) based on the principle that the stronger does not follow the weaker.
5
Confirm the evidence
The text references hadith collections (Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah, etc.) that support the principle, although the exact proof is not quoted. The ruling is well-established in the Hanafi school.
The chain of authority is from Zahir al-Riwayah, making it the relied-upon position.

And the reason the following of the clothed by the naked is not permissible is because the naked is missing a condition of prayer, while the clothed is capable of it, so he is in a higher state than him. Commentary
This passage explains the ruling that a clothed person should not follow a naked person in prayer. The 'naked' here means one who does not have clothing to cover the obligatory parts (awrah). The 'clothed' has the covering, which is a condition for the validity of prayer. Since the clothed person is in a higher state (able to fulfill the condition), he cannot follow the naked person who is deficient in that condition. The principle of 'the strong cannot follow the weak' applies here as well.

Key Terms
عار (aari): A person who does not have clothing to cover the obligatory parts (awrah) in prayer.
لابس (labis): A person who has clothing covering the required parts for prayer.
شرط الصلاة (shart al-salah): A condition for the validity of prayer; covering the awrah is one such condition.

A clothed person cannot follow a naked person in prayer because the naked lacks the condition of covering the awrah, and the clothed, by fulfilling it, is in a higher state that cannot descend to follow the lower.

The Principle of Strong Not Building on Weak: The Qari and Ummi Case
Commentary on the principle and the first case of qari leading ummi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of the strong not building on the weak in prayer and apply it to determine the invalidity of a qari following an ummi.

Core Idea
The imam is a guarantor; the follower's prayer is built upon the imam's, so a stronger follower cannot build on a weaker imam.

This lesson unpacks the foundational principle that governs many imamate rulings in the Hanafi school. We begin with the key hadith 'al-imam damin' and examine its application to the case of a person who knows Quran (qari) being led by one who does not (ummi). The commentary explores the definition of ummi and the rare exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The principle is that the strong cannot build on the weak (la yajuz bina' al-qawi 'ala al-da'if). This is derived from the hadith 'al-imam damin'.
2
Understand the evidence
The hadith 'al-imam damin' indicates that the imam's prayer encompasses the follower's. A thing cannot contain what is above it, only what is like or below it.
3
Define the key terms
'Ummi' is defined as one who does not know enough Quran to make prayer valid: one verse (Abu Hanifa) or three short verses (Sahibayn). 'Qari' is the opposite.
Intra-madhhab difference is important.
4
Apply to the case
Since the qari is stronger in recitation, his prayer cannot be built on the ummi's deficient recitation. The follower's prayer is invalid.
The hukm level is batil.
5
Note exceptions
If the qari is at the door or adjacent to the mosque, the ummi's prayer alone is valid by consensus. If both are in the same mosque in different corners, there is dispute.
Al-Qadi Abu Khazim reported a riwayah from Malik allowing it.

And a reciter (led by) an illiterate person, and a clothed person (led by) a naked person. The basis in this matter and similar matters is the hadith: 'The imam is a guarantor,' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi with a sound chain; for it indicates that the prayer of the imam includes the prayer of the follower, and it is known that a thing does not include what is above it, but rather includes what is like it or below it. Hence the principle of our scholars is the impermissibility of the strong building on the weak, and they derived many branches from it, and they answered reports that indicate otherwise in particular cases with multiple answers, and the place of their elaboration is 'al-Si'aya.' [1] His statement: 'And a reciter (led by) an illiterate person'—the meaning of 'illiterate' as mentioned by Taj al-Shari'ah in Sharh al-Hidayah and others is: one who does not memorize from the Quran what suffices for his prayer, which is one verse according to him (Abu Hanifa) and three short verses according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and the meaning of 'reciter' is the opposite. He is called illiterate because he is as if his mother gave birth to him in ignorance and lack of memorization. The reason for this is that the illiterate person is incapable of recitation, which is one of the pillars of prayer, while the reciter is capable of it. So if he abandons the obligation of recitation while being capable, his prayer becomes invalid. It is mentioned in al-Muhit: 'If the reciter is at the door of the mosque or adjacent to the mosque, and the illiterate person is in the mosque praying alone, then his prayer is valid by consensus. Likewise, if the reciter is in a prayer other than the illiterate person's prayer, it is permissible for the illiterate person to pray alone and not wait for the reciter to finish. But if the reciter is in one corner of the mosque and the illiterate person in another corner and their prayers are concurrent, then al-Qadi Abu Khazim mentioned: According to the analogy of Abu Hanifa's statement, the illiterate person's prayer is not valid, and this is the statement of Malik, and in a narration: it is valid.' [2] His statement: 'And a clothed person (led by) a naked person'—the meaning of 'naked' is one who does not have a garment covering what must be covered in prayer, and 'clothed' is the opposite. The reason that the clothed person's following of the naked person is not permissible is that the naked person lacks a condition of prayer, while the clothed person is capable of it, so he is in a higher state. Thus in al-Ghunyah. 101 His statement: 'Because following is a partnership'—the summary is that following is a partnership with the imam, so it is necessary that they agree in the description of the prayer, for disagreement destroys the partnership. [6] His statement: 'And the imam should not prolong it'—meaning the prayer or the recitation; i.e., to the extent that it causes the people boredom and disruption, due to the hadith: 'Whoever leads a people, let him pray with them the prayer of the weakest among them, for among them are the old...' Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on a matn from al-Wiqaya listing cases where the imam must be equal or stronger than the follower in a specific capacity. The core principle is that the strong cannot build on the weak (la yajuz bina' al-qawi 'ala al-da'if), derived from the hadith 'al-imam damin'—the imam is a guarantor. This means the follower's prayer is built upon the imam's; if the imam is deficient, the follower's prayer is also deficient. The passage examines six cases: (1) A reciter (qari) cannot be led by an illiterate person (ummi) who cannot recite the required minimum. The definition of ummi differs—Abu Hanifa requires one verse, the Sahibayn require three short verses. The commentary notes exceptional scenarios where this is allowed, such as when the reciter is outside the mosque. (2) A clothed person cannot be led by a naked person (aari) who lacks covering. (3) A person who prays with full bowing and prostration cannot be led by one who prays with gestures (mumi) due to incapacity. (4) A person praying an obligatory prayer (mufarrid) cannot be led by one performing a supererogatory prayer (mutanafill). (5) A person praying one fard cannot be led by one praying a different fard (e.g., Zuhr behind Asr). (6) The imam should not prolong the prayer to the extent of causing hardship. The commentary also addresses a potential objection: if disparity invalidates following, why is it allowed for a supererogatory performer to follow an obligatory performer? The answer is that the supererogatory is absolute (mutlaq) and can be absorbed into the obligatory, while the obligatory is restricted and cannot be absorbed into the supererogatory.

Key Terms
أمي (ummi): A person who does not memorize enough Quran to make his prayer valid; defined as knowing less than one verse (Abu Hanifa) or less than three short verses (Sahibayn).
قارئ (qari): One who knows the required Quran recitation for prayer, i.e., at least one verse (Abu Hanifa) or three short verses (Sahibayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it not valid for a qari to be led by an ummi in congregational prayer?
A) Because the ummi cannot say the takbir.
B) Because the qari's prayer is built on the ummi's deficient recitation, violating the principle that the strong cannot build on the weak.
C) Because the ummi is not a valid Muslim.
D) Because the ummi cannot lead any prayer at all.

Key Takeaway
A qari cannot be led by an ummi; the follower's prayer is invalid because the strong cannot build on the weak.

Four Additional Cases: The Strong Cannot Follow the Weak
Commentary on the remaining cases and the prohibition of prolonging.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four additional cases where the strong cannot follow the weak in prayer and explain the rationale for each.

Core Idea
Being clothed, able to bow/prostrate, praying fard, and praying the same fard are strengths that cannot be built upon weaker counterparts.

This lesson continues applying the principle of the imam as a guarantor to four more scenarios from the text. Each case illustrates that the follower must not be stronger than the imam in a relevant capacity: covering, physical ability to perform ruku and sujud, the type of prayer (fard vs. nafl), and the specific fard being prayed. We also cover the prohibition of prolonging the prayer.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle
The principle is that the strong cannot build on the weak (la yajuz bina' al-qawi 'ala al-da'if). This governs all these cases.
2
Identify the capacity in each case
For each case, pinpoint the capacity where the imam is weaker: (1) covering (clothed vs. naked), (2) physical ability (full ruku/sujud vs. gestures), (3) type of prayer (fard vs. nafl), (4) specific fard (e.g., Zuhr vs. Asr).
3
Understand the exception
Note that the reverse is allowed: a mutanafill (nafl) can follow a muftarid (fard) because nafl is absolute and can be absorbed into fard.
This is mentioned in the commentary.
4
Apply the prohibition on prolonging
The imam should not prolong the prayer to the point of hardship, based on the hadith about praying with the weakest in mind.

And a reciter (led by) an illiterate person, and a clothed person (led by) a naked person. The basis in this matter and similar matters is the hadith: 'The imam is a guarantor,' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi with a sound chain; for it indicates that the prayer of the imam includes the prayer of the follower, and it is known that a thing does not include what is above it, but rather includes what is like it or below it. Hence the principle of our scholars is the impermissibility of the strong building on the weak, and they derived many branches from it, and they answered reports that indicate otherwise in particular cases with multiple answers, and the place of their elaboration is 'al-Si'aya.' [1] His statement: 'And a reciter (led by) an illiterate person'—the meaning of 'illiterate' as mentioned by Taj al-Shari'ah in Sharh al-Hidayah and others is: one who does not memorize from the Quran what suffices for his prayer, which is one verse according to him (Abu Hanifa) and three short verses according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and the meaning of 'reciter' is the opposite. He is called illiterate because he is as if his mother gave birth to him in ignorance and lack of memorization. The reason for this is that the illiterate person is incapable of recitation, which is one of the pillars of prayer, while the reciter is capable of it. Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya is a commentary on a matn from al-Wiqaya listing cases where the imam must be equal or stronger than the follower in a specific capacity. The core principle is that the strong cannot build on the weak (la yajuz bina' al-qawi 'ala al-da'if), derived from the hadith 'al-imam damin'—the imam is a guarantor. This means the follower's prayer is built upon the imam's; if the imam is deficient, the follower's prayer is also deficient. The passage examines six cases: (1) A reciter (qari) cannot be led by an illiterate person (ummi) who cannot recite the required minimum. The definition of ummi differs—Abu Hanifa requires one verse, the Sahibayn require three short verses. The commentary notes exceptional scenarios where this is allowed, such as when the reciter is outside the mosque. (2) A clothed person cannot be led by a naked person (aari) who lacks covering. (3) A person who prays with full bowing and prostration cannot be led by one who prays with gestures (mumi) due to incapacity. (4) A person praying an obligatory prayer (mufarrid) cannot be led by one performing a supererogatory prayer (mutanafill). (5) A person praying one fard cannot be led by one praying a different fard (e.g., Zuhr behind Asr). (6) The imam should not prolong the prayer to the extent of causing hardship. The commentary also addresses a potential objection: if disparity invalidates following, why is it allowed for a supererogatory performer to follow an obligatory performer? The answer is that the supererogatory is absolute (mutlaq) and can be absorbed into the obligatory, while the obligatory is restricted and cannot be absorbed into the supererogatory.

Key Terms
مومئ (mumi): One who prays with gestures (ishaarah) due to inability to bow and prostrate.
متنفل (mutanafill): One performing a supererogatory (nafl) prayer.
عار (aari): A person who does not have clothing covering the required parts of the body for prayer.

In four additional scenarios, the strong cannot follow the weak: clothed/naked, non-gesturing/gesturing, fard/nafl, and different fards.

The Three Prohibitions in Congregational Prayer Leadership
Explaining prohibitions on who may lead in congregational prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three prohibited scenarios in congregational prayer leadership and explain the rational proof for each, including the exception for a supererogatory follower behind an obligatory imam.

Core Idea
Building the strong upon the weak is impermissible; partnership in prayer description requires unity.

In congregational prayer, the imam and follower must share a certain parity. This passage from al-Wiqayah outlines three scenarios where leadership is invalid due to disparity in ability, obligation type, or prayer description. Understanding these rules prevents common errors in jam'ah and clarifies when following is valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibited cases
From the matn, extract each prohibition: (1) a non-gesturer behind a gesturer, (2) a muftarid behind a mutanaffil, (3) a muftarid behind someone performing a different fard.
These are all cases of disparity between imam and follower.
2
Understand the rational proofs
The proofs are given: 'building the strong upon the weak is invalid' (for case 1) and 'following is a partnership requiring unity' (for cases 2 and 3). Analyze why these rationales apply.
The proofs are aqli (rational), not directly from nass.
3
Note the exception and its justification
The objection asks: why can a mutanaffil follow a muftarid? The answer is that supererogatory prayer is absolute and can be built upon any valid prayer, whereas the muftarid is restricted.
This exception is crucial for practical application.

[2]قوله : بمتنفّل ؛ سواء كان مصليّاً للسنن الراتبة أو غيرها ؛ لأنَّ الاقتداء عبارة عن متابعة شخص آخر في أفعاله بصفاتها ، ووصفُ الفرضية معدوم في حق الإمام، فلا يتحقق البناء عليه. كذا في «البناية»(۳).
(۳) قوله : لأن ... الخ الظاهر أنه دليل بما يقارنه، وهو عدم جواز اقتداء المفترض بالمتنقل، ويمكن أن يكون دليلاً لجميع المسائل السابقة، فإنّ هذا المعنى مرعي في كلّها. [٤]قوله : ومفترض ؛ عطف على متنفّل ؛ أي لا يقتدي مفترض بمَن يُصلِّي فرضاً غير فرضه : كمصلّي الظهر بمصلّي العصر، ومصلّي قضاء العصر بمصلّي قضاء الظهر، ولا يصح اقتداء الناذر بالناذر ، إلا إذا نذر الثاني عين ما نذره الأول. كذا في «الغُنية». 101 قوله : لأن الاقتداء...الخ ؛ حاصله : أنّ الاقتداء شركة مع الإمام، فيلزم أن يتوافقا في وصف الصلاة، فإنّ المخالفة تفوّت الشركة.
فإن قلت : فيلزم أن لا يجوز اقتداء المتنفّل بالمفترض أيضاً لوجود التخالف. قلت : لا تخالف هناك، فإنّ النفل مطلق ، والفرض مقيد.
Book of Prayer, Chapter on Congregation. And not a person praying by gesture leading one who prays by gesture, and one performing an obligatory prayer leading one performing a supererogatory prayer, and one performing an obligatory prayer leading another performing a different obligatory prayer, and the imam should not prolong it. (And not a person praying by gesture leading one who prays by gesture, and one performing an obligatory prayer leading one performing a supererogatory prayer) because building the strong upon the weak is not permissible. (And one performing an obligatory prayer leading another performing a different obligatory prayer) because following is a partnership, so unity is required. [1] His saying: 'And not a person praying by gesture': meaning that one who does not pray by gesture (i.e., he bows and prostrates standing or sitting) may not be led by one who cannot do that and prays by gesture, because the condition of the follower is stronger than the imam. As for one praying by gesture following another such person, it is permissible without disagreement, due to their equality, even if one gestures while standing and the other while sitting. As in 'al-Binayah'. [2] His saying: 'by one performing a supererogatory prayer': whether he is praying the regular sunnah or other supererogatory prayers, because following is defined as following another person in his actions with their attributes, and the attribute of obligation is absent in the imam, so building upon it is not realized. As in 'al-Binayah'. (3) His saying: 'because...etc.' It appears that it is evidence for what accompanies it, namely the impermissibility of one performing obligatory prayer following one performing supererogatory prayer, and it can be evidence for all the preceding issues, for this meaning is observed in all of them. [4] His saying: 'And one performing an obligatory prayer': conjuncted to 'one performing a supererogatory prayer'; meaning it is not permissible for one performing an obligatory prayer to be led by someone praying a different obligation, such as a Zhuhr worshiper being led by an 'Asr worshiper, and a person making up 'Asr being led by a person making up Zhuhr. And it is not valid for one who has vowed to follow another who has vowed, unless the second vowed the exact same thing as the first. As in 'al-Ghunyiah'. 101 His saying: 'because following...etc.' The summary is that following is a partnership with the imam, so they must agree in the description of the prayer, for disagreement nullifies the partnership. If you say: then it would follow that it is also not permissible for one performing supererogatory prayer to follow one performing obligatory prayer due to the presence of disparity. I say: there is no disparity there, for supererogatory is absolute, while obligatory is restricted.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Wiqayah, expanded by the commentator. It addresses three prohibitions in congregational prayer leadership. First, a person who prays with full bowing and prostration (standing or sitting) cannot be led by someone who only gestures (mumi) because the follower is physically stronger in action; building the strong upon the weak is invalid. If both gesture, it is valid regardless of their posture. Second, a person performing an obligatory prayer (muftarid) cannot be led by someone performing a supererogatory prayer (mutanaffil), because the attribute of obligation is absent in the imam, so the follower cannot build his obligatory prayer on the imam's supererogatory action. Third, a muftarid cannot be led by another muftarid doing a different obligatory prayer (e.g., Zhuhr behind 'Asr); because following is a partnership in the prayer's description, and differing in the type of obligation breaks that partnership. A possible objection: why can a mutanaffil follow a muftarid? The answer is that supererogatory prayer is absolute and can be built on restricted obligatory prayer, but not vice versa.

Key Terms
مومئ (mumi'): A person who prays by gestures (pointing) due to inability to bow or prostrate.
مفترض (muftarid): One performing an obligatory prayer (fard).
متنفل (mutanaffil): One performing a supererogatory (nafl) prayer.
اقتداء (iqtida'): Following the imam in congregational prayer.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership; in this context, the follower and imam share in the prayer's description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it impermissible for a person performing an obligatory prayer to follow someone performing a different obligatory prayer (e.g., Zuhr behind 'Asr)?
A) Because the imam's prayer is not valid.
B) Because following requires partnership in the description of the prayer, and differing types of obligation break that unity.
C) Because the follower must be stronger than the imam.
D) Because the imam’s intention is not valid.

Key Takeaway
A valid congregation requires parity between imam and follower in capacity, obligation category, and specific prayer type, except that a supererogatory follower may follow an obligatory imam.

Imam's Prolongation and Dispute on First Rak'ah in Fajr
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on first rak'ah length

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on imam's prolongation of prayer and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifah/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad regarding the first rak'ah in Fajr.

Core Idea
The imam should not prolong prayer to cause hardship; the first rak'ah is lengthened only in Fajr to allow latecomers.

This passage from the matn of al-Hidayah addresses two interlinked rulings: the imam's duty to avoid lengthening the prayer excessively, and a specific sunnah regarding the first rak'ah in Fajr. The commentator explains the rationale and cites a dispute between the founding Hanafi imams on whether this sunnah applies only to Fajr or to all prayers. Understanding this nuance helps the student appreciate how evidence is weighed within the school.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling on imam's prolongation
Identify the hadith cited and the principle: the imam must not make the prayer so long as to cause weariness. This is a general sunnah (recommended) based on prophetic practice.
The hadith uses the imperative 'falyusal', which in Hanafi usul indicates recommendation, not obligation.
2
Identify the specific exception in Fajr
Note the phrase 'except in Fajr'. The commentator explains the reason: it is a time of sleep, so lengthening the first rak'ah helps people catch it.
This exception applies only to Fajr according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf restrict the lengthening to Fajr, citing the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri about Zuhr and 'Asr. Muhammad extends it to all prayers, citing the hadith of Abu Qatadah.
The dispute is about the scope of the sunnah: does the first rak'ah have a special status in every prayer or only in Fajr?
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The matn's wording 'except in Fajr' indicates the relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. This is supported by the majority of later Hanafi authorities.
The tarjih is based on the strength of the dalil and the public benefit (maslahah) of not prolonging prayers generally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإمام لا يطيلها
[٦] قوله : والإمام لا يطيلها ؛ أي الصلاة أو القراءة ؛ أي بحيث يورث القوم ملالاً واختلالاً ؛ لحديث: «مَن أمّ قوماً فليصل بهم صلاة أضعفهم ، فإن فيهم الكبير والمريض وذا الحاجة ) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم، وفي رواية: «إذا صلى أحدكم بالناس فليخفف ، فإنّ فيهم الضعيف والسقيم والكبير، وإذا صلى لنفسه فليطوّل ما شاء (۲).
ولا قراءة الأولى إلا في الفجر
[١] قوله : إلا في الفجر ؛ لأنه وقت نوم وغفلة، فيطول الأولى ؛ لكي يدرك الناس الركعة الأولى، ولا كذلك في سائر الصلوات وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف (۳) ، ويشهد لهما ما رواه مسلم وغيره عن أبي سعيد الخدري : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقرأ في الركعتين الأوليين من الظهر قدر ثلاثين آية، وفي الأُخْرَيَين قدر خمس عشرة آية، وفي العصر في الأوليين قدر خمس عشرة آية، وفي الأخريين قدر نصف ذلك»(4). وعند محمد له يطوّل الأولى في الصلوات كلّها لما رواه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما من حديث أبي قتادة : «إنّ النبي الله كان يقرأ في الركعتين الأوليين من الظهر بفاتحة
And the imam should not prolong it.
[6] His saying: 'And the imam should not prolong it': i.e., the prayer or the recitation, i.e., such that it causes the people weariness and disorder; due to the hadith: 'Whoever leads a people in prayer, let him pray with them the prayer of the weakest among them, for among them are the elderly, the sick, and the one with a need.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim. And in another narration: 'When one of you prays with the people, let him be brief, for among them are the weak, the ill, and the elderly. And when he prays alone, let him prolong as he wishes.'
And the first recitation is only prolonged in Fajr.
[1] His saying: 'Except in Fajr': because it is a time of sleep and heedlessness, so he lengthens the first rak'ah so that people may catch the first rak'ah. It is not like that in other prayers. This is according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. And what supports them is what Muslim and others narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'The Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr about thirty verses, and in the last two about fifteen verses; in 'Asr in the first two about fifteen verses, and in the last two about half of that.' And according to Muhammad, he lengthens the first rak'ah in all prayers, due to what Bukhari and Muslim and others narrated from the hadith of Abu Qatadah: 'The Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr with the Fatihah...'
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues: the imam's duty not to prolong the prayer excessively, and the specific ruling on recitation length between the first and second rak'ahs. The matn states that the imam should not lengthen the prayer. The commentator explains this means the imam should not make the recitation so long as to cause hardship for the congregation, citing the famous hadith that the imam should pray with the weakest in mind. The second part of the matn says that the first recitation (in the first rak'ah) is only prolonged in the Fajr prayer. The commentator notes that this is the position of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, who argue that in Fajr, because it is a time of sleep and heedlessness, a longer first rak'ah allows latecomers to catch the first rak'ah. They support this with evidence from the practice of the Prophet in Zuhr and 'Asr where the first two rak'ahs are not longer than the last two (in fact, the first two are longer in Zuhr, but the hadith shows equal distribution? Actually the hadith shows the first two are longer than the last two, but the point is that it's not specific to first vs second rak'ah but to the set). However, Muhammad argues that the first rak'ah should be lengthened in all prayers, basing it on the hadith of Abu Qatadah that the Prophet used to recite a long passage in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr. The dispute centers on whether the emphasis on the first rak'ah applies to Fajr only or to all prayers. The text does not definitively state the mufta bihi position, but the matn's phrasing 'except in Fajr' suggests the relied-upon view is that of Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
يُطيلها (yutīluhā): Prolonging the prayer or recitation to an extent that causes hardship for the congregation.
القراءة الأولى (al-qirā'ah al-ūlā): The recitation in the first rak'ah of a prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All five daily prayers.
B) Fajr only.
C) Zuhr and 'Asr.
D) Maghrib and 'Isha.

Key Takeaway
The imam should not prolong prayer to cause hardship, and the sunnah of lengthening the first rak'ah is specific to Fajr according to the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Lengthening the First Rak'ah in Fajr Only
Intra-Hanafi dispute on imam lengthening first rak'ah only in Fajr vs all prayers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on lengthening the first rak'ah and evaluate the intra-madhhab evidence of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf vs Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
The imam lengthens the first rak'ah only in Fajr, not all prayers, to allow latecomers to join.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the imam should lengthen the first rak'ah only in Fajr or in all prayers. The base text states the exception of Fajr, and the commentator explains the reasoning and the differing view of Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute helps clarify the operative ruling (mufta bihi) and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling and its rationale
Identify the ruling: the first rak'ah is lengthened only in Fajr. The reason given is that Fajr is a time of sleep and heedlessness, so the imam lengthens to allow people to catch the first rak'ah.
This is the starting point of the commentary.
2
Identify the two positions within the Hanafi school
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that lengthening is only in Fajr. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that it is in all prayers. Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Examine the evidence for each position
The text cites two hadiths: Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (for Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf) shows the Prophet recited fewer verses in later rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr, implying no exceptional lengthening. Abu Qatada (for Muhammad) explicitly mentions lengthening the first rak'ah in Zuhr, Asr, and Fajr.
Both are authentic hadiths from Bukhari/Muslim.
4
Understand the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Muhammad, in all prayers the first rak'ah is longer; according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, only in Fajr.
The difference affects imams leading prayer.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (only Fajr). This is supported by the majority of later scholars.
Authority weight: Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا قراءة الأولى إلا في الفجر
قوله : إلا في الفجر ؛ لأنه وقت نوم وغفلة، فيطول الأولى ؛ لكي يدرك الناس الركعة الأولى، ولا كذلك في سائر الصلوات وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف، ويشهد لهما ما رواه مسلم وغيره عن أبي سعيد الخدري : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يقرأ في الركعتين الأوليين من الظهر قدر ثلاثين آية، وفي الأُخْرَيَين قدر خمس عشرة آية، وفي العصر في الأوليين قدر خمس عشرة آية، وفي الأخريين قدر نصف ذلك». وعند محمد له يطوّل الأولى في الصلوات كلّها لما رواه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما من حديث أبي قتادة : «إنّ النبي الله كان يقرأ في الركعتين الأوليين من الظهر بفاتحة الكتاب وسورتين، وفي الأُخْرَيَين بفاتحة الكتاب، ويطول في الركعة الأولى ما لا يطيل في الثانية، وهكذا في العصر، وهكذا في الصبح فظننا أنه يريد بذلك أن يدرك الناس الركعة
And there is no lengthening of the first [rak'ah] except in Fajr. He said: 'Except in Fajr' because it is a time of sleep and heedlessness, so he lengthens the first [rak'ah] so that people may catch the first rak'ah, and not so in other prayers. This is according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf, and their evidence is what Muslim and others narrated from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr about thirty verses, and in the last two about fifteen verses; and in Asr, in the first two about fifteen verses, and in the last two about half of that.' And according to Muhammad, he lengthens the first [rak'ah] in all prayers because of what Bukhari and Muslim and others narrated from the hadith of Abu Qatadah: 'The Prophet, peace be upon him, used to recite in the first two rak'ahs of Zuhr the Opening of the Book and two surahs, and in the last two the Opening of the Book, and he would lengthen the first rak'ah more than the second, and likewise in Asr, and likewise in Fajr. We thought that he intended by that to let people catch the rak'ah.'
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether the imam should lengthen the first rak'ah only in Fajr prayer or in all prayers. The base text states that there is no lengthening of the first rak'ah except in Fajr. The commentator explains that the reason is that Fajr time is a time of sleep and inattentiveness, so the imam lengthens to allow people to join the first rak'ah. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and they support it with the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Prophet read shorter amounts in the later rak'ahs of Zuhr and Asr. Muhammad, however, holds that the first rak'ah should be lengthened in all prayers, based on the hadith of Abu Qatada that the Prophet lengthened the first rak'ah in Zuhr, Asr, and Fajr. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, limiting the practice to Fajr.

Key Terms
الأولى (al-ula): The first rak'ah of the prayer.
الصلوات (al-salawat): The five daily prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, in which prayer does the imam lengthen the first rak'ah?
A) All five prayers
B) Only Fajr
C) Fajr and Maghrib
D) Zuhr and Asr

Key Takeaway
The imam lengthens the first rak'ah only in Fajr prayer to allow latecomers to join; this is the operative Hanafi position (mufta bihi).

Positioning a Single Follower to the Right and the Imam's Step Forward
Commentary on the positioning of followers in congregational prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on positioning a single follower in congregational prayer and explain when the imam steps forward according to the preferred view.

Core Idea
A single follower stands to the imam's right; when a second joins, the imam steps forward—this is sunnah and easier.

This lesson examines a key sunnah of congregational prayer: the positioning of followers relative to the imam. The source passage, from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, explains that a single follower should stand on the imam's right, and if more followers join, the imam steps forward rather than ordering them to move back. Understanding this ruling helps us preserve the Prophetic practice and avoid common mistakes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for a single follower
The matn states a single follower (mu'tam) is positioned to the imam's right. This is a sunnah practice.
The commentary explains the imam gives the order, indicating authority.
2
Locate the proof
The commentary cites the hadith of Ibn Abbas in Bukhari and Muslim where the Prophet turned him from left to right.
This is a dalil naqli showing the Prophet's action.
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'yataqaddam'
The commentary gives two scenarios: stepping forward during the prayer when a second follower joins, or stepping forward at the start if there are multiple followers.
The commentator prefers the first for ease.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf holds that with two followers, the imam stands between them; the text states the most correct (asahh) view is stepping forward.
The majority view is the operative opinion.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, the imam steps forward rather than ordering followers to step back, especially in crowded congregations.
This prioritizes ease (yusr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقيم مؤتماً توحد عن يمينه، ويتقدم إن زاد
أي إذا كان المؤتم واحداً يأمره الإمام بأن يقوم عن يمينه ، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الإمام آمر، والمأموم مأمور يجب أن يكون منقاداً له.
ويتقدم إن زادَ فيه إشارة إلى أن القومَ إذا كانوا كثيراً ، فالأولى أن يتقدم الإمام لا أن يأمرهم الإمامُ بالتأخير عنه ، فإن ذلك أيسر من هذا.
[1] قوله : يأمره الإمام ; أي بالقول خارج الصلاة أو بفعل منبه له داخل الصلاة ، كما رواه البخاري ومسلم : «أنّ النبي قام للتهجد، فقام معه ابن عباس عن شماله، فأداره رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن يمينه).
[٢] قوله : ويتقدم ; هذا يحتمل محملين :
الأول : أن يكون معناه يتقدّم الإمام إذا زاد المقتدي على الواحد في أثناء الصلاة، فإنّ الإمام إذا كان معه واحد وقام على يمينه وشرع في الصلاة، ثم جاء مقتد ثان، فحينئذ إما أن يتأخر المقتدي، ويصفّ معه خلف الإمام، أو يتقدم الإمام من موضعه ويخلي موضعاً ليصف الجائي مع المؤتم، وهذا هو الأولى.
والثاني : أن يكون معناه أنه إذا كان المقتدى زائداً على الواحد عند الشروع في الصلاة تقدم عليه، وعلى هذا حمله الشارح البارع . [۳] قوله : كثيراً ; المراد به ما فوق الواحد ; فإنّ المقتدي إذا كان اثنين يتقدم الإمام أيضاً على الأصح ، وعند أبي يوسف في هذه الصورة يتوسطهما. [٤]قوله : أيسر ; لا سيما إذا كان القومُ جماً غفيراً مزدحمين ففي تأخيرهم كلفة.
And he makes a single follower stand to his right, and he steps forward if [the followers] increase. That is, if the follower is one, the imam orders him to stand on his right, and therein is an indication that the imam is the commander and the follower is commanded, and must be submissive to him. And 'he steps forward if they increase' is an indication that when the people are many, it is preferable that the imam steps forward rather than the imam ordering them to step back, for that is easier than this. [1] He said: 'The imam orders him'—meaning verbally outside the prayer or by a gesture inside the prayer, as narrated by Bukhari and Muslim: 'The Prophet stood for tahajjud, and Ibn Abbas stood with him on his left, so the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, turned him to his right.' [2] He said: 'And he steps forward'—this has two interpretations: First, it means the imam steps forward when a follower exceeds one during the prayer. If the imam has one follower standing on his right and they begin the prayer, then a second follower comes, then either the follower steps back and forms a row with him behind the imam, or the imam steps forward from his place and vacates a spot for the newcomer to line up with the follower—this is preferable. Second, it means that if at the start of the prayer the followers number more than one, he steps forward. The teacher al-Bari' has adopted this interpretation. [3] He said: 'many'—meaning more than one. If there are two followers, the imam also steps forward on the most correct view; according to Abu Yusuf in this case, he stands between them. [4] He said: 'easier'—especially if the congregation is large and crowded, for ordering them to step back involves hardship.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains the sunnah positioning of followers in congregational prayer. The matn states that a single follower (mu'tam) stands to the imam's right, and if there are more than one, the imam steps forward. The commentator elaborates that the imam orders the single follower to stand on his right, indicating that the imam directs and the follower obeys. Regarding 'stepping forward,' the commentator presents two interpretations. The first: if during the prayer a second follower arrives, either the first follower steps back to form a row behind the imam, or the imam steps forward to make room; the latter is preferable. The second interpretation: if at the start there are multiple followers, the imam leads from the front. The text also mentions that if there are two followers, the imam steps forward on the most correct view, though Abu Yusuf holds that the imam stands between them. The commentary concludes that stepping forward is easier, especially when the congregation is large.

Key Terms
مؤتم (mu'tam): A follower in congregational prayer.
توحد (tawahhada): To be alone, single (referring to a single follower).
يتقدم (yataqaddam): To step forward (the imam moving ahead when followers increase).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should the imam do if a single follower is praying with him, and then a second follower joins during the prayer?
A) The first follower steps back to form a row with the second
B) The imam steps forward
C) The second follower stands next to the first on the right
D) The imam orders both to step back

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling prioritizes ease: the imam steps forward rather than ordering followers to move back.

Positioning of the Follower in Congregational Prayer
Ruling on follower positioning with evidence and intra-Hanafi variant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the positioning of followers in congregational prayer, identify the hadith evidence, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement about two followers.

Core Idea
A single follower stands to the imam’s right; when two or more, the imam steps forward — this is sunnah, not fard.

This lesson explains the proper positioning of followers relative to the imam in congregational prayer according to the Hanafi school. The ruling is derived from the practice of the Prophet ﷺ, especially the hadith of Ibn Abbas. We will also explore an important intra-Hanafi difference concerning the case of exactly two followers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the positioning of the follower(s) relative to the imam in jama'ah. Determine the hukm level: is it fard, wajib, sunnah, etc.?
The text indicates it is sunnah, based on the Prophet's practice.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proof cited for the single follower standing to the right. The text quotes the hadith of Ibn Abbas being turned from left to right.
The dalil is a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Distinguish the two scenarios
The text discusses two situations: one follower, and multiple followers (more than one). The ruling differs: one follower stands to the right; imam steps forward when there are two or more.
The phrase 'ويتقدم إن زاد' indicates the imam steps forward when they increase.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf holds that when there are exactly two followers, the imam stands between them rather than stepping forward. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) say he steps forward.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the imam steps forward.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a real-life situation: you arrive at the mosque and find only one follower already in prayer with the imam. Where should you position yourself? According to the Hanafi ruling, you should step back and form a row behind the imam, or the imam may step forward if you join during prayer.
The text mentions two possibilities: the follower can step back, or the imam can step forward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي إذا كان المؤتم واحداً يأمره الإمام بأن يقوم عن يمينه ، وفيه إشارة إلى أنَّ الإمام آمر، والمأموم مأمور يجب أن يكون منقاداً له.
ويتقدم إن زادَ فيه إشارة إلى أن القومَ إذا كانوا كثيراً ، فالأولى أن يتقدم الإمام لا أن يأمرهم الإمامُ بالتأخير عنه ، فإن ذلك أيسر من هذا.
[1] قوله : يأمره الإمام ؛ أي بالقول خارج الصلاة أو بفعل منبه له داخل الصلاة ، كما رواه البخاري ومسلم : «أنّ النبي قام للتهجد، فقام معه ابن عباس عن شماله، فأداره رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن يمينه).
[٢] قوله : ويتقدم ؛ هذا يحتمل محملين :
الأول : أن يكون معناه يتقدّم الإمام إذا زاد المقتدي على الواحد في أثناء الصلاة، فإنّ الإمام إذا كان معه واحد وقام على يمينه وشرع في الصلاة، ثم جاء مقتد ثان، فحينئذ إما أن يتأخر المقتدي، ويصفّ معه خلف الإمام، أو يتقدم الإمام من موضعه ويخلي موضعاً ليصف الجائي مع المؤتم، وهذا هو الأولى.
والثاني : أن يكون معناه أنه إذا كان المقتدى زائداً على الواحد عند الشروع في الصلاة تقدم عليه، وعلى هذا حمله الشارح البارع . [۳] قوله : كثيراً ؛ المراد به ما فوق الواحد ؛ فإنّ المقتدي إذا كان اثنين يتقدم الإمام أيضاً على الأصح ، وعند أبي يوسف في هذه الصورة يتوسطهما. [٤]قوله : أيسر ؛ لا سيما إذا كان القومُ جماً غفيراً مزدحمين ففي تأخيرهم كلفة.
Meaning: When the follower is one, the imam commands him to stand on his right, and therein is an indication that the imam is the commander, and the follower is the commanded, obligated to be submissive to him. 'And he steps forward if they increase' in it is an indication that when the people are many, it is better for the imam to step forward rather than commanding them to step back from him, for that is easier than this. [1] His statement: 'He commands him' meaning either by speech outside the prayer or by a prompting action within the prayer, as Bukhari and Muslim narrated: 'The Prophet stood for tahajjud, and Ibn Abbas stood with him to his left, so the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, turned him to his right.' [2] His statement: 'And he steps forward' has two possible interpretations: First: it means the imam steps forward when the follower increases during the prayer. If the imam has one follower standing to his right and they start the prayer, then a second follower comes, either the follower steps back and lines up behind the imam, or the imam steps forward from his place and vacates a place for the newcomer to line up with the follower; this is preferable. Second: it means if the followers are more than one at the start of the prayer, the imam steps forward; the commentator al-Bari’ adopted this. [3] His statement: 'many' means more than one; if there are two followers, the imam also steps forward according to the most correct view, but according to Abu Yusuf in this situation he stands between them. [4] His statement: 'easier' especially if the people are a large crowd jostling, for ordering them to step back involves hardship.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on the positioning of followers in congregational prayer according to the Hanafi school. The main text states that if there is one follower, he stands to the imam's right, and the imam commands him (by word or gesture). If there are more than one, the imam steps forward instead of making the followers move back, as that is easier. The commentary expands on this with the hadith of Ibn Abbas turning from left to right. It also discusses two interpretations of 'step forward': either during prayer if another joins, or at the start. A notable intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned: according to Abu Yusuf, when there are exactly two followers, the imam stands between them rather than stepping forward. The relied-upon position (al-ashah) is that the imam steps forward even with two.

Key Terms
مؤتم (mu'tamm): The follower in congregational prayer, also called مقتدي.
مقتدي (muqtadi): The follower who prays behind the imam.
اليمين (al-yamin): The right side of the imam; the single follower stands here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The sunnah positioning in jama'ah depends on the number of followers: one stands to the right; more than one prompts the imam to step forward.

Ruling When the Imam's Hadath Becomes Apparent After Prayer
Ruling that followers must repeat if imam's hadath is discovered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the imam's hadath is discovered after prayer and explain the dalil and the companion dispute.

Core Idea
The imam's impurity invalidates the followers' prayer — both must repeat, based on the principle al-imamu damin.

Imagine you have just completed prayer behind an imam, and afterward you learn that he was unknowingly in a state of ritual impurity. Are your prayers valid? The Hanafi school has a clear ruling on this, derived from the practice of Ali (رضي الله عنه) and the hadith 'al-imamu damin'. Let us examine the proof and the companion disagreement that shaped this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (the imam's hidden hadath) and what the consequence is for the followers (they must repeat).
The ruling is that i'ada (repetition) is wajib for both imam and followers.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited: the athar from Ali (the followers must repeat) and the principle 'al-imamu damin'.
Muhammad recorded the athar in Kitab al-Athar; also narrated by Abd al-Razzaq.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the evidence proves the ruling: the imam's prayer 'encompasses' the followers' prayer (tadamun), so if his is invalid, so is theirs. The 'imamu damin' hadith reinforces that the imam's state is linked to the followers' validity.
4
Note the companion ikhtilaf
Recognise that Umar (رضي الله عنه) held a different view: he repeated but did not ask the followers to repeat. Ali disagreed, saying they should also repeat.
The Hanafi school follows Ali's view, considering it stronger in evidence.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi school prefers the view that followers must repeat: the principle of tadamun and the explicit athar from Ali outweigh the isolated practice of Umar.
The mufta bihi is that both imam and followers repeat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ظهر حدثه يعيد المؤتم؛ لأنَّ صلاة الإمامِ متضمن صلاة المقتدي، ففسادُهُ يُوجِبُ فساده.
[1] قوله : ولو ظهر حدثه ؛ أي ظهر أنّ الإمام كان محدثاً بالحدث الأصغر أو الأكبر تجب على المؤتم أيضاً الإعادة هو المروي عن علي الله أنه قال في الرجل يصلّي بالقوم جنباً قال : «يعيدون»، أخرجه محمد له في «كتاب الآثار». وروى عبد الرزاق : «أنّ عليَّاً صلّى بالناس وهو جنب أو محدث فأعاد وأمرهم أن يعيدوا»، وروى أيضاً : «أنّ عمر الله صلّى بالناس وهو جنب فأعاد ولم يعيدوا، فقال علي : قد كان لمن صلى معك أيضاً أن يعيدوا ) ، ويؤخذ ذلك من حديث : «الإمام ضامن»، كما أشار إليه الشارح ، فإنّ الإمام إذا كان ضامناً لصلاة المؤتم، وتضمنت صلاته لصلاته لا جرم تصح بصحتها وتفسد بفسادها.
And if his hadath appeared, the follower repeats; because the imam's prayer encompasses the follower's prayer, so its invalidity necessitates its invalidity. [1] His statement: 'And if his hadath appeared' i.e. it becomes apparent that the imam was in a state of minor or major hadath; then the follower is also obligated to repeat. This is narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said regarding a man who prayed with people while in janabah: 'They should repeat.' Muhammad recorded it in Kitab al-Athar. Abd al-Razzaq narrated that Ali prayed with people while in janabah or hadath, so he repeated and ordered them to repeat. He also narrated that Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) prayed with people while in janabah, so he repeated but they did not repeat. Ali said: 'Those who prayed with you should also repeat.' And this is derived from the hadith 'The imam is a guarantor', as the commentator indicated, for if the imam is a guarantor for the follower's prayer, and his prayer includes their prayer, then it necessarily is valid by its validity and invalid by its invalidity.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when it becomes known after the prayer that the imam was in a state of ritual impurity (minor or major). The Hanafi ruling, based on the narration from Ali (رضي الله عنه), is that both the imam and the followers must repeat their prayer. The reasoning is that the imam's prayer incorporates the followers' prayer (tadamun), so if the imam's prayer is invalid, so is theirs. The commentary cites the principle 'al-imamu damin' (the imam is a guarantor) as a basis. There is also a reported difference between Ali and Umar (رضي الله عنهما) on this point, but the Hanafi school follows the view that the followers must repeat.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity, either minor (حدث أصغر) or major (حدث أكبر).
الإمام ضامن (al-imamu damin): A hadith principle meaning the imam is a guarantor for the follower's prayer; used to derive rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Both the imam and the followers repeat their prayer
C) The prayer is valid for both because they were unaware
D) The followers repeat but not the imam

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that if the imam's hadath is discovered after prayer, both imam and followers must repeat, based on the hadith 'al-imamu damin' and the athar of Ali (رضي الله عنه).

Order of Rows in Congregational Prayer: Men, Boys, Khunatha, Women
Ruling on the sequence of rows: men first, then boys, then khunatha, then women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi order of rows in congregational prayer and identify the supporting hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Men closest to the imam, then boys, then khunatha, then women – a sunnah order based on maturity and the Prophet's practice.

In congregational prayer, the arrangement of rows is not arbitrary; the Hanafi school prescribes a specific sequence based on hadith and the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains that men should be at the front, followed by discerning boys, then khunatha (hermaphrodites), and finally women at the back. Understanding this order helps you appreciate the sunnah and the wisdom behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, recognize that the passage gives the preferred arrangement of rows in congregational prayer. The ruling is that this order is a sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah), not an obligation (fard). The prayer is valid even if the rows are mixed.
The hukm level: sunnah muakkadah.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The passage cites two hadiths: (1) 'Let those who are mature and sensible stand nearest to me' (ليليني منكم أولوا الأحلام والنهى), and (2) 'Delay them as Allah has delayed them' (أخروهن من حديث أخرهن الله). Find these in the commentary.
The word 'ahlam' means puberty, 'nuha' means intellect.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (proof direction)
Explain how each hadith supports the order. The first positions mature men near the imam. The second places women at the back. Boys, being intermediate, go between.
The illa (reason) is maturity (hilm) and intellect ('aql).
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Some other schools may not distinguish between boys and khunatha, or may place women directly behind men without the intermediate categories. Recognize this as a legitimate difference.
The Hanafi position is based on explicit hadith and the practice of a Sahabi.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The Hanafi school adopts this order as the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position because of the clear hadith and the action of Abu Malik al-Ash'ari (RA) as narrated by Ahmad.
The authority is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويَصُفُ الرّجال ، ثم الصبيان ، ثم الخناثا.
[٢] قوله : ثم الصبيان ؛ وذلك لأنَّ الرجال أحق بالتقديم، وقرب الإمام يدل عليه حديث: «ليليني منكم أولوا الأحلام والنهى ) ، والأحلام جمع حلم بالضم، وهو البلوغ، والنهي بالضم جمع نهية بمعنى : العقل. والنساء أحق بالتأخير الحديث: «أخروهن من حديث أخرهن الله» (۳) كما مر. والصبيان في المرتبة الوسطى، فيكونون بينهما، وقد روى أحمد عن أبي مالك الأشعري الله أنه قال: ألا أريكم صلاة رسول الله، فصلى بالناس وصف الرجال، ثم الولدان، ثم النِّساء».
And the men are arranged in rows, then the boys, then the khunatha. [2] His statement: 'then the boys' because men are more deserving of being forward, and closeness to the imam is indicated by the hadith: 'Let those of you who are mature and sensible stand nearest to me' – 'ahlam' is the plural of 'hulm' meaning puberty, and 'nuha' is the plural of 'nuhya' meaning intellect. And women are more deserving of being delayed by the hadith: 'Delay them as Allah has delayed them' as previously mentioned. Boys are in the middle rank, so they are between them. Ahmad narrated from Abu Malik al-Ash'ari (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'Shall I show you the prayer of the Messenger of Allah?' So he prayed with the people and arranged the men, then the boys, then the women.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on the order of rows in congregational prayer. The preferred sequence is: first men, then boys (who have not reached puberty but are discerning), then khunatha (hermaphrodites), then women. The commentary explains that men are placed closest to the imam because of their maturity and intellect, citing the hadith 'let those who are mature and sensible stand near me'. Women are placed at the back, following the hadith 'delay them as Allah has delayed them'. Boys occupy the middle position. The practice of the Prophet as demonstrated by Abu Malik al-Ash'ari is also cited as evidence.

Key Terms
الصبيان (al-sibyan): Boys who have not reached puberty but are old enough to understand prayer.
الخناثا (al-khunatha): Hermaphrodites; those with ambiguous gender. They are placed between boys and women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Men, then boys, then khunatha, then women
C) Boys, then men, then women
D) Men, then khunatha, then boys, then women

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi sunnah for rows in jama'ah is men, then boys, then khunatha, then women; the prayer remains valid even without it.

Imam's Hidden Impurity: Follower Must Repeat
Ruling and proof for follower repeating prayer when imam's impurity is discovered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an imam's hidden impurity is discovered after prayer and explain the proof from the hadith of Ali (رضي الله عنه).

Core Idea
If the imam was impure, the follower's prayer is invalid and must be repeated.

Imagine you pray behind an imam, and after the prayer you learn he was in a state of major impurity (janabah). Does your prayer count? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position: the follower must repeat because the imam's prayer legally encompasses the follower's. We will examine the ruling, its proof from the hadith of Ali (رضي الله عنه), and the underlying principle that the imam is a guarantor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the follower's prayer after discovering that the imam was in a state of minor or major impurity (hadath). The ruling is that the follower must repeat (tajibu al-i'adah).
The text uses 'يعيد' (yuid) which indicates obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith of Ali (رضي الله عنه) narrated in Kitab al-Athar: 'He said concerning a man who prays with people while junub: They repeat (يعيدون).' Also a similar narration from Abd al-Razzaq.
The hadith is ahad, which in Hanafi usul gives a zanni proof, resulting in a wajib (not fard) obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit command 'يعيدون' indicates obligation. Additionally, the principle 'al-imam damin' (the imam is a guarantor) links the imam's prayer to the follower's; if the imam's prayer is invalid, the follower's is also invalid.
The passage states: 'فإن الإمام إذا كان ضامناً لصلاة المؤتم... تفسد بفسادها'.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage mentions that Umar (رضي الله عنه) repeated his prayer but did not order the followers to repeat. Ali (رضي الله عنه) held that followers must repeat. The Hanafi school follows Ali's ruling.
This shows that even among the Companions there was a difference, but the stronger evidence supports the followers' obligation.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school prefers Ali's explicit command and the general principle of guarantee over Umar's practice. Thus the mufta bihi is that followers must repeat.
In later fatwa, this ruling is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ظهر حدثه يعيد المؤتم؛ لأنَّ صلاة الإمامِ متضمن صلاة المقتدي، ففسادُهُ يُوجِبُ فساده.
[١] قوله : ولو ظهر حدثه ؛ أي ظهر أنّ الإمام كان محدثاً بالحدث الأصغر أو الأكبر تجب على المؤتم أيضاً الإعادة هو المروي عن علي الله أنه قال في الرجل يصلّي بالقوم جنباً قال : «يعيدون»، أخرجه محمد له في «كتاب الآثار». وروى عبد الرزاق : «أنّ عليَّاً صلّى بالناس وهو جنب أو محدث فأعاد وأمرهم أن يعيدوا»، وروى أيضاً : «أنّ عمر الله صلّى بالناس وهو جنب فأعاد ولم يعيدوا، فقال علي : قد كان لمن صلى معك أيضاً أن يعيدوا ) ، ويؤخذ ذلك من حديث : «الإمام ضامن»، كما أشار إليه الشارح ، فإنّ الإمام إذا كان ضامناً لصلاة المؤتم، وتضمنت صلاته لصلاته لا جرم تصح بصحتها وتفسد بفسادها.
If his impurity becomes apparent, the follower repeats, because the imam's prayer encompasses the follower's prayer, so its corruption necessitates his corruption. [1] His statement: 'If his impurity becomes apparent' means it becomes apparent that the imam was in a state of minor or major impurity; the follower is also obligated to repeat. This is narrated from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said concerning a man who prays with people while in a state of janabah: 'They repeat.' Muhammad narrated it in Kitab al-Athar. Abd al-Razzaq narrated that Ali prayed with people while junub or impure, so he repeated and ordered them to repeat. And it is also narrated that Umar prayed with people while junub, so he repeated but they did not repeat. Ali said: 'Those who prayed with you should also repeat.' This is derived from the hadith 'The imam is a guarantor' as the commentator indicated. For if the imam is a guarantor for the follower's prayer, and his prayer encompasses the follower's prayer, necessarily it is valid by its validity and corrupted by its corruption.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage states a ruling and provides its proof. The ruling: if it is discovered after the prayer that the imam was in a state of impurity (whether minor or major), then the followers must repeat their prayer. The reason is that the imam's prayer legally encompasses the follower's prayer; as the imam is a guarantor (ضامن) for the follower's prayer. If the imam's prayer is invalid due to his impurity, the follower's prayer is also invalid and must be repeated. The proof is a narration from Ali (رضي الله عنه) that he ruled for a man who led the prayer while junub, that both the imam and followers must repeat. This is supported by the hadith 'The imam is a guarantor.'

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): Ritual impurity, whether minor (حدث أصغر) or major (حدث أكبر).
مؤتم (mu'tam): Follower in congregational prayer (muqtadi).
جنب (junub): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If it is discovered after prayer that the imam was in a state of janabah (major impurity), what is the Hanafi ruling for the followers?
A) Their prayer is valid and they do not need to repeat.
B) They must repeat the prayer because the imam's prayer encompasses theirs.
C) Only the imam repeats, the followers are fine.
D) They must repeat only if they knew the imam was impure.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that if the imam's hidden impurity is discovered after prayer, the followers must repeat because the imam's prayer legally encompasses theirs, based on the hadith of Ali and the principle that the imam is a guarantor.
